Medical.
Dr, RICHARDS.
The new ELECTRICAL, CONSULTING and
LABORATORY ROOMS being completed, Dr.
Richards will in future be pleased to welcome hia
friends and patients, during his consulting hours,
at his new address,
34 COLLEGE STREET, SYDNEY.
1
$
: .
□
This eminent Specialist, Founder, and Chief
Surgeon of the long-established and favourably-
known Dispensary that now bears his name, re-
ceived his medical education in the leading colleges
and hospitals of Europe. He has devoted a life-
time to the study of
PRIVATE, NERVOUS, AND SKIN
DISEASES.
He has by profound research* thoroughly investi-
gated their origin, traced their deadly sources and
insidious dissemination, and ascertained the surest
and safest methods by which they may be eradi-
cated, with these widely recognised qualifications
combined with unswerving faithfulness to his
patients, he has during his practice, extending
over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment. His practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.
YOUNG MEN
who suffer from the results of early follies, trans-
gressions or excesses of more nature age, bladder
difficulties, a whitish or dark ropy sediment in
the water, accompanied by slight burning or-
smarting sensation, Headaches, Drowsiness during
the day, circles round the eyes, Giddiness,
irregularity of the bowels, specks before the eyes,
bashfulness, discontentedness, weakness or pains
in the back, Liver or kidney complaints, dreams,
buzzing noises in the ears and head, pimples, a
fear that something dreadful is about to happen,
weak stomach, yellow or blood-shot eyes, impure
blood, hasty, uncontrollable temper, hacking
cough, who cannot marry, nervousness in society,
wakefulness, palpitation of the heart, general
weakness, and other symptoms which lead to in-
sanity and death, should consult him, for he has
mastered these difficulties. On receipt of 6d. in
stamps he will send secure from observation in a
plain wrapper, postage
free, a large cloth-bound
BOOK.
Showing plainly how all diseases of the private or
nervous system may be cured thoroughly and per-
manently without unnecessary expence. This
book will prove a valuable companion, and even
though you be perfectly well its general inform-
tion and prescription may assist you in restoring
some poor friend to health even after hia case has
been pronounced incurable by his medical at-
tendant.
ONE POUND IS SUFFICIENT TO
SEND
for Remedies necessary for the cure of NEB
VOUS DEBILIT Y. Should you require medical
treatment, as procrastination is sometimes dan-
gerous, it would be advisable for you to sit down
quietly in your chamber and write to the doctor,
detailing every experienced symptom. He will
on receipt of such a letter, write yon, promptly
and courteously, a candid and straightforward
oponion of your case. If you do not think you
understand the case, send for his famous medical
work, the price of which is but sixpence ; enclosed
in the volume will be found a l ! st of carefully-pre-
pared questiohs, which will, in all probability,
assist you materially in describing your ailment.
j) r Richards uses nothing but the purest remedies,
and never prescribes mercury under any circum-
stances. The moment letters are carefully per-
used and answered they are destroyed by fire,
and patients may rest assured that their cases
will remain a secret between the doctor and
themselves. Dr. Richards has successfully treated
nearly 25,000 cases since coming to Australia, and
has permission from at least 10,000 persons to
show their written evidence of his success. All
communications should be addressed
Dr. RICHARDS,
34 COLLEGE STREET, SYDNEY.

Specialist for the treatment of this class of disease, but,
unfortunately for the suffering public in Australia the
apathy shown by Qualified Men in the treatment of
Chronic and Nervous Complaints has induced men of the
lowest grade of intellect (whose only qualifications are
unlimited assurance) to spring up, and by specious
advertisements and false promises trap the unwary.
It is very evident that sufferers from these diseases
have been scandalously treated by these unqualified
persons The evidence taken at tire Medical Enquiry
held in Sydney (published in the Daily Telegraph, Nov.
Gth 7th and Bth, 1890) plainly shows the disgraceful
manner in which patients are duped. Not only are
they robbed of their money, but what is of infl.dtely
greater Importance, their health is injured also. It is
therefore time that legally Qualified Men entered the
field to fight these pests of society with their own
weapons, viz., Advertising.
It is utterly impossible for those who are suffering
Horn the.e diseases, who are scattered over all parts of
Australasia, to ascertain which Qualified Practitioner
makes these diseases his speciality unless he advertises.
I consider therefore, that by advertising I not o.ly
strike a Mow at the Quackery which now exists, but I also add
to the usefulness of my profession ami in no way lessen my
personal dignity.
_ My Qiiiiliilcntlons I Invito Iho fullest investigation into. For some considerable time I was House Surgeon to the
Great Northern Hospital, London, where I had special opportunities of observin ; the many phases of the Nervous amt
Sexua, System,and to become acquainted with the most scientific methods of effecting their cure.
My sole attention la given to the treatment of these diseases, and when a person consults me he is Informed at I lie
if°l undertak™the c° ther 1 tan effeot his oure or not ‘ The P atient is not deluded with false arid specious promises, and
I Absolutely Guarantee a Complete and Permanent Cure.

“AND USE THEM IF NECESSARY.”
“ BRISBANE, Thursday.—Evidence is accumulating that the strike is fast approaching a close. The Colonial Secretary has received a letter from the country cadet
corps expressing disgust at the recent cowardly acts of the unionist shearers and offering to-shoulder their rifles, and use them if necessary. "—Daily paper.

SATURDAY, APRIL 18, 1801.
The Consummation of the Crime.
When reviewing, a month ago, the debate
in the Federal Convention on the resolutions
framed by Sir Henry Parke,s— a debate
which was useless on resolutions which were
considered merely to sop an old man’s petu-
lant vanity— The Bulletin declared that if
the Conventionists did not reach in their
later discussions a higher level of patriotism
and intelligence, they could hardly help
consummating a crime. That crime has
been consummated. The Conventionists
have sold the liberties of the Australias, and
now ask the people to ratify the scandalous
transaction.
# # #
Owing its origin to the strategic party-move
of a man whose grasp of power in New
South Wales was being fretted away by the
scandals and follies of his administration,
and who was under the necessity, therefore,
of diverting the popular mind—a man who
is a cunning Parliamentarian and an ornate
rhetorician and nothing more—a man who
has been swept into office, time and again,
by the accursed waves of sectarian hate,
who has never passed a law that he did not
crib, and never advocated a principle that
he did not before denounce or afterward
betray ; a man who has never manifested the
faintest symptom of constructive genius or
genuine nobility of soul, and whose solitary
title to be termed a statesman rests upon his
capacity to put his opponents in the
wrong ; a man who is so little as to
endure the smile of aristocrats who publicly
insult him in what should be his dearest
relationships ; a man who feels proud because
the Times, organ of Pigotism and slander,
s dutes him with a lie—a lie founded upon its
own preposterous ignorance—owing its origin,
we say, to this political paralytic, the Conven-
tion was discredited from the moment of its
conception. It was net created by the
people ; it arose from no enthusiastic popular
sentiment ; it was not preceded by the
labour-throes which herald the birth of a
strong vitality ; it was not originated in the
necessity of events. We said this before,
tßough we were reluctant to do so, because,
as our readers know, we have toiled as no
other paper has toiled to unite the Austra-
lias in a Federal Union, and were prepared
to welcome any form of Federation that
promised permanence. We say it again ;
not with reluctance this time, but with
bitterness and sorrow with bitterness
against the men who could so abase the
noblest opportunity which ever fell to the
lot of politicians ; and with grief that the
“great men ” of these provinces have proved
themselves so contemptibly small that they
have sacrificed their one chance of immor-
tality for the petty distinctions to be con-
ferred by a fast-falling Throne.
# # *
There is no doubt about it. This is no
time to mince words or to soften to the ear
a brutal strength of phrase. If the Conven-
tionists spoke truly when they one and all
averred the magnificence of their task, then
smooth, pretty-sounding syllables are not
wanted in this discussion. If the work be
great and worthy of men in their intellectual
and moral prime, let it be criticised with
virility of tone and strength of passion. If
the Constitution is to dominate a Common-
wealth, not of four millions of souls, but of
ten, twenty, forty, a hundred millions—and
this is the alleged hope and aim of the men
who framed it—there is no demand for the
employment of the elegancies of language
and the “tinsel clink of compliment ” till it
is seen whether the praise is deserved. What
is wanted is the cool steel of logic, and then,
if need be, the bludgeon of invective.
We do not hesitate to declare that
there is no doubt about the influence,
the pernicious, traitorous influence, exerted
over the Convention by the fact of the
Imperial Connection and the probability
of Imperial “honors.” The members of
the Convention had an absolutely clean sheet
before them whereon to write a Constitution
which should have asserted to the crack
o’ doom the sanctity- of human liberty and
the dignity of the individual man. It would
have been an accident of the situation that,
with the immortal document itself which
might have transcended in worth the Declara-
tion of Independence by so much as that
transcends Magna Chart a, their own names
would have gone down to the grateful ages,
but the accident would have conferred a dis-
tinction besides which the Knighthood of
the Round Table itself would pale its bril
liancy. And, now, what has been done?
The Conventionists have written on the
sheet words which will be erased by the
keen edge of the Australias’ scorn, and they
have bartered the reverence and applause of
the mighty Future —not that of -Australia
alone, but that of the World—for the smile
of a Prince— such a Prince ! —and the two-
fingered shake of his local agents.
# # #
Tliis is their shame and their crime. They
cheered the Queen at the conclusion of the
final session, but they did not cheer the
Commonwealth. Even their eifrontery did
not go that far. The omission was, perhaps,
trifling, but as a straw shows the direction
of the stream, and the thistle-down the
impulse of the wind, the incident indicated
indubitably the trend of the Conventionists’
thoughts and aspirations. Not on the Aus-
tralias were tin ir eyes fixed ; but on the
foreign throne which is to grant the
baronetcies, and the Privy Councillorships,
and the knighthoods, and in their legislative
result they succeeded beyond the measure
which most men receive when they strive to
make their work coincide with their wishes.
All through the Convention, the Australian
and the democratic element was steadily
voted down ; all through it, the Imperialists
had their own way. The falsities and the
shams which cannot be separated from
Imperialism without death to it as to theili-
selves are to be implanted in the Common-
wealth, and in it is to be perpetuated the
social system, which is the product of the
aristocratic idea —a system which is based
upon class-wrong and class-villainy,. upon
privilege and wealth, and upon the emascu-
lated humanity which is a condition of the
existence of caste, privilege, wealth. The
Conventionists proposed to call into being a
new nation. They have done their best to
endow 7 with life a thing which will reproduce
in time the scrofulous sores of the Old World.
# * *
Look at the record. Obey, whose pure
and honourable past should have commanded
respect if his words in the present could not
win assent, jeei’ed at and insulted for his
repeated attempts to give the people an
absolute voice in the framing of machin-
ery which is to control their destiny ;
Kingston pityingly patronised, as one
patronises a crank or a child, for his effort
to constitute an industrial judiciary, the most
rational and well-considered scheme yet
promulgated by any Australian politician to
meet the social problem ; Cockbubn ridi-
culed for his democracy, and reminded that
his personal interests would not be served
by his claim that one man should have one
vote and no more ; Downer contemptuously
smiled upon as, paraphrasing The Bulletin,
he prophesies discord between the States
and the Federal Government. These on one
side ; and on the other— Fitzgerald, the
beer-drawer, denouncing Cockburn’s sugges-
tion as “ impudence • Griffith, with the
assertion that “ every prerogative of the
Throne not retained by her Majesty w 7 as
vested in the Governor-General ” —a state-
ment which is true, but which is
damnable, inasmuch as its plain sig-
nificance is that the Federal Government
will be far less free and far more dependent
on the Crown than any one Australian pro-
vince is at the present day ; Munro, with
22 Victorian votes In his pocket, bestowing
verbal approval of the “ One man one vote ”
principle, and repudiating it on division ;
Playford, with his expressed hope that the
Constitution will be submitted to the people,
and his laughter at the idea of eliciting the
people’s will on the details of the measure:
“ How can you expect an intelligent decision
from the electors ?” ho asked, in effect ;
Gillies doing his best to disfranchise the
electors who had put him into political
power, and who had paid his bread and
meat bill that he might have leisure
to learn how he could betray them ;
and Parkes —the President, whose “ p
solutions ” were literally tossed contemp-
tuously aside at the first meeting of the
Constitutional Sub-Committee, Parkes, who
got down from the Chair to pour out hog-
wash and vituperation. These on the other.
On the one side, the force of manhood and
the Australian conscience ; on the other, the
power of Imperialism and Caste, of the Beer-
vat and the Plural Vote, of the Banker’s
pass-book and its correlative, the Insolvency
Schedule. Need we say which side was in
the .majority ? There was a loud chorus of
“ Ayes ! ” when the Constitution as a whole
was put to the vote. Sir George Grey’s
voice rang out in a solitary “No!” The
coming generations are going to vote with
Grey, though now it may be as President
Parkes declared, “ That the Ayes have it !”
* * *
Look further. You will find in the Con-
stitution no clause to secure, at any stage of
the progress of legislation, the effect of the
popular will. It is a written Constitution —
and almost every clause will have to be
interpreted by precedent—British precedent
—or by reference to that other and non-
written Constitution —the British ! The
popular, the Australian will is never pro-
vided for ; the Imperial will always. Time
and the popular judgment have been filing
away the Imperial connection to a shadow
but the Constitution lias renewed its sub-
stance. Note the vicious circle ; Legislatuics
elected by the plutocratic and, therefore,
privileged vote, are to enact the Constitu-
tion ; the same vote will elect (he Fedeial
Parliament under the Constitution ; from the
Parliament thus erected there are to be selec-
ted Ministers of State ; these Ministers are
to be chosen by the Croivn, and it will be
through those Responsible Officers of the
Crown that the life of the Constitution is to
to express itself. Will a Ministry so chosen
from a House so elected do aught to affect
the Imperialistic and privileged interests of
which it is the creature ? It would be
expecting the impossible and giving the lie
to all our knowledge of politicians, to suppose
that anything of the sort could happen. The
Federal Executive will be the voice, not of
the People, but of the Classes and the Crown.
A majority vote in the House of Represen-
tatives would not necessarily drive Ministers
from office, because it would rest with the
Governor-General to retain them in power,
and if the occasion of the vote were a ques-
tion of the Imperial relations, it would be,
manifestly, the Viceroy’s unquestionable
duty to keep them as “ his advisers.” The
Constitution, in times of political conflict,
will give to a Viceroy who is strong in self-
confidence and in the support of the Crown,
an enormous power which has no analogy in
British institutions, properly so-called. Its
analogue is the Viceroy-ship of India, which
is the supreme authority of a conquered
country. The Bulletin asserts that the
closer the relation of the Federal Parliament
to the Executive, and the relation of
the Executive to the Governor - General
and to the Crown are studied, the more
apparent it will be that a fatal conces-
sion has been made by the Constitution
to Imperialism. That the result will be
ultimate good we believe, but the path to it
will be marked with evil. The popular will
must rule in the end, but, if the Constitution
remain as it is, the end will only be reached
after deadlocks and suffering. Had the Con-
ventionists steered for the Australian ideal,
or had they even' been content to leave
unassailed the liberties already enjoyed by
the Provinces, the end would have come
naturally and without violence.
# # #
The Constitution must not be allowed
t > remain as it is. This paper was the
first to demand that the Constitution
should be submitted in detail to the
popular will. There is very little ques-
tion now that this claim will be sup-
ported in the several State Parliaments. If
the Constitution be placed before district or
State Conventions in detail, we shall be as
content as if a referendum on particulars
were, made to the whole people, always pro-
viding that the State Convention is elected
by the adult male vote. Every male of
legal majority must have a vote for
the local Convention, whether a registered
elector of his State or not. The plan
would be novel, no doubt, but so is the
machinery of the State Convention. Then,
from the State Conventions must go back to
the State Parliaments the Constitution as
affirmed, or amended, or rejected ; an,d from
the several State Legislatures', it iiiiS.be
sent on to a second and, if need be, a third:
Federal Convention. Jill The Bulletin
affirmed the need of a second Convention in
its last issue, the possibility of holding one
never occurred to the delegates, and it was
not till then that the majority strenuously
exerted themselves to demonstrate that if
another Convention is held, there will be no
finality to the job of Constitution-making.
There never will be finality, and for that
reason there is no hurry to begin, or, having
begun, to prosecute the work. Had the
Conventionists framed a Constitution which
provided the essential condition of per-
manency —elasticity of machinery to give
effect to the people’s will—their work,
humanly speaking, would have been final.
But in their eagerness to protect Im-
perialism, and to win their rewards, they
have betrayed the Australians, and the Aus-
tralians would .be unworthy to possess a
free, self-made Constitution if they do not
decline, by refusing to give their decision in
haste, to imperil their national greatness at
the bidding of the creatures of Imperialism.
Judges’ Sentences.
Ip any other body of men administering public
affairs delivered, on open evidence, judgments
of such manifestly irrational disparity as those
daily dispensed, by the occupants of the New
South Wales and District Court benches, it is
beyond doubt that the people would long ago
have risen in indignation and demanded either
that the travesty on commonsense should be
restrained or a radically different system in-
augurated. The embezzlement boom through
which the country is now passing, appears to
have afforded New South Wales judges a special
opportunity of placing prominently on record
that they care nothing for one another’s
opinions, nothing for the opinion of the public
and nothing for the prisoner’s sense of what is
an equitable average punishment for his crime.
Some may think this last a small matter. Tire
approval or disapproval of a, convicted em-
bezzler may be regarded as of lit tip consequence;
but we contend that sufficient thought will
show that, taken in relation to social reform,
its importance cannot be over-estimated. A
criminal’s natural tendencies in difficulties
are towards the avenues of his original
sin, and the man who remembers that for em-
bezzling a small sum he was punished more
severely than another for embezzling -a large
one will not, at his second fall, steal simply to
meet a need, but will take all he can lay hands
on to.meet the possibilities of judicial caprice.
6 * #
If Judges cared for one another’s opinions,
the wide discrepancies between their sentences
for similar offences would have long ago caused
them to meet in council and determine an
approach to a common basis of thought as to
punitive methods ; and if they regarded public
sentiment otherwise than as a thing to spurn,
they would make some effort to allay the shame
and irritation which the public feel when they
find one member of the bench rioting savagely
among pains and penalties to overwhelm the
embezzler of a few pounds and another cour-
teously despatching for a brief recess the em-
bezzler of tens of thousands. A few well-known
recent cases will put our meaning clear. At the
Sydney Quarter Sessions the other day Judge
Backhouse gave six years to bank-manager
Bradley, whose defalcations admittedly
amounted to £20,000 or over, and whose proven
embezzlements came to £6040 ; while Judge Fos-
ter, at Hay, sentenced Land Agent Sidney H.
Haynes, whose total defalcations amounted to
£l6O, to eight years’ hard labour! The very
same week Judge Innes sentenced John
Morgan Dkuce, whose embezzlements amounted
to £5OOO, to four years’ penal servitude, while
Judge Windeyer gave John Dodd Beckett
two years and a-half for embezzling £6B 10s.
Where, w r e would ask, is the line of reason in
these diverse conceptions of punitive justice ?
It is true that though offences be equal the
habitual criminal merits heavier punishment
than the criminal novice, and under shelter of
the supposition of such past character, judicial
vagaries often escape unscathed. But, taking
Haynes and Bradley, Haynes and Laser -
touche, Haynes and Druce, and Haynes and
any other of the recent victims of the embezzl-
ing mania, the Hay and Balranald land-agent’s
character, public and private, was equal to that
of any of them. No allegation of any previous
impropriety was produced against him, and evi-
dence was established that when arrested he was
preparing to gradually refund to the Treasury
the sums he had misappropriated or ‘ ‘ advanced
to himself.” Now, why should Haynes have
got eight years for £l6O, and Bradley only six
years for the proven £6040, and Druce only four
for the proven £5OOO ? These figures may be
placed in a row and looked at from many
points, but we confidently assert that there is
no point of view from which they do not con-
stitute an arraignment of judicial capacity to
apportion sentences.
* # *
This brings us to our old contention. The
jury which declares a man guilty should declare
how far lie is guilty. The discretion as to
sentences now given to judges should be given
to the juries. A juror, like a judge, may err;
he may indulge, like a judge, in the luxuries of
the table, and approach his duty next morning
with a torpid liver and congested blood-vessels ;
he may, like a judge, have found when young
that wine made him sleepy, and thus cultivated
a habit of bilious abstemiousness at feud with
the requirements of his gastric juices ; and, as
a man is the creature of his secretions, he may
in the performance of duty exhibit therefore
malignant severity or indulge in weak pity.
But there is this to be said of that juror—he
will have eleven others to correct him. A man
might as well be tried by one juror as sen-
tenced by one judge. Trial by one’s peers covers
the whole ground of acquittal and punishment,
and to this view society must come. Until this
point is reached the interposition of the judge
will continue to be a spectacle of power anoma-
lously exercised and of personal caprice which
goads commonsense to revolt.
“Failed to get Evidence.”
The Victorian authorities are holding an official
inquiry into the conduct of a certain constable
named O’Callaghan, who is charged with gross
neglect of duty, inasmuch as he “ failed to get
evidence.against, Margaret Ryan,” and should
the charge of failing to get evidence lie sub-
stantiated, Mr. O’Callaghan will probably
slide down that steep track which does not lead
to promotion. The accusation brought against
Margaret Ryan was one of homicide, and to
the disgust of an intelligent and cultured nation
it fell through. A good many months ago Mr.
Ryan, husband of the prisoner, was found,
either drunk or insensible or both, on the read
some distance from his home, and when he died
shortly afterwards the prevailing impression
was that Ids crust had been rent asunder
through falling on one of those eight-pound
masses of blue-metal with which an intelligent
department loves to repair the highways ot
Australia. Mrs. Ryan was looked at by the
sagacious eye of the law to see if her conduc
could throw any further light on the matter,
but she was absent when the event took place,
and apparently quite undisturbed, and there
were no brains or gore about her, and no visibt 0


symptoms of a deadly conflict, so the matter
dropped for the time. After a while, however,
some new suspicions were aroused, and the
widow was arrested, but owing to the neglect
of Constable O’Callaghan in failing to get
evidence against her she was acquitted, oo tai
the case is sufficiently simple, and can be
summed up in three propositions: (l)loliceman
O’Callaghan was sent out to gather sufficient
information to hang Elizabeth Ryan ; (2) he
didn’t get the information; (3) Elizabeth
Ryan was not hanged, as a natural consequence.
Whether there was, or was not, any such in-
formation in existence as the authorities wanted,
nobody can tell. The charge against O’Calla-
ghan simply sets forth that he failed to get
evidence, whether it was there or not, and if ho
comes to grief, he will serve as a warning and
an example to the next officer who goes out on
a similar errand. The police force is naturally
divided into three classes—the first consisting
of policemen who get evidence that actually
exists; the second, of policemen who get evi-
dence anyhow; and the third, of policemen who
don’t get evidence, possibly because there isn’t
any to get; and this latter class are a horror
and an aggravation to their superiors, and a
disgrace to the traditions of the force. And as
the O’Callaghan plainly belongs to the third
category, he is not likely to be a success in that
s( a .ion of life to which Providence has called
him. An officer who lets a really good and
interesting hanging fall through for want of
evidence is a failure.
# * *
The case, however, presents another aspect.
If Mr. O’Callaghan had been a sufficiently
energetic man, and had accumulated enough
shady and dubious evidence, or any other kind
of evidence, to hang Mrs. Ryan, it would then
have been the business of that prisoner’s
solicitor, if she had one, to hunt up testimony
on the other side. If he neglected to do so, it
is a matter of absolute certainty that the police
authorities would not have taken any proceed-
ings against him for “ failing to get evidence.”
If the evidence was waiting for him in the
next street, and he declined to go there and
look for it because there wasn’t enough money
in the job, or because his corns were troubling
him, or on account of the weather, Mrs. Ryan
would probably have been hanged for no other
reason than that her legal representative was
too much exhausted to go round the corner.
If he hired a cheap and idiotic counsel, on the
other hand, and this individual neglected to
make use of the evidence when it was found,
nobody would have instituted an inquiry into
that circumstance either. Or if the counsel
failed to turn up at all—if he merely collected
his fee and stayed away, and left Mrs. Ryan
to toss up with Providence whether she would
hang or not—the police would certainly not
take any steps to make that legal gentleman sit
up for his conduct. There is plainly abundant
legal machinery to squash Constable O’Cal-
laghan if he “failed to get evidence” against
a prisoner, but there is none at all to squash
anybody who fails to get evidence for the de-
fence of the same prisoner. Consequently,
O’Callaghan, or any other individual in the
same situation, is likely to get evidence in
future with the vigour of a man whose billet is
at stake, and with the calm confidence of a
policeman who knows that the prisoner’s lawyer
has no such incentive, and consequently is not
likely to create much trouble.
* * *
If the prisoner has no lawyer and no money
the path of the typical O’Callaghan is made
still smoother for his large and weary feet. In
that case the prisoner has to get his own evi-
dence, and as he is locked up and can’t get
evidence at all, he has to leave the matter
entirely to others, who, as a rule, leave it alto-
gether. No one can prosecute the prisoner for
“failing to collect testimony” on his own
behalf, or any such charge, because the first
sign that he has neglected his duty in this
important matter consists in the Judge sen-
tencing him to be hanged, and it is obviously
impossible to hang a man and prosecute him as
well. A man who is going to be hanged is
impervious to imprisonment or penal servitude,
and unless he is flogged for not gathering facts
for his own defence, and then hanged for want
of the facts, he can’t be reached on this charge
at all. But as the prisoner himself is safely
deposited out of sight and hearing, the logical
course is obviously to prosecute all his friends
and relations for not getting the necessary
information on his behalf, and unless this is
done the course of justice is lopsided and un-
even. The next O’Callaghan may fill up his
case with lies and glaring perjury rather than
risk being ejected from his situation and forced
to sell fish for a livelihood, and nobody on the
other side has any sufficient incentive to burst
up his assertions. The accused, of course,
has incentive enough, but as he is planted
in a secure stone place as if he were
coal, and is not allowed to give way to
any natural craving to be on O’Callaghan’s
track, he does not count. When an inactive
and somnolent officer can be prosecuted for not
proving Mrs. Ryan was guilty it follows that —
if that same officer had proved her guilt—some-
one on the other side should have been pro-
ceeded against for not demonstrating her inno-
cence. If the man who conducts the prosecu-
tion is fighting for his position and all his future
prospects, and the man who conducts the de-
fence is only fighting for a fee which will be
paid just the same even if he stays in bed all
through the trial, the proceedings are ridicu-
lously uneven.
* * *
It is one of the noblest features of the Aus-
tralian judicial system that no person goes un-
defended on a capital charge. In some of the
provinces that cheap and useless barrister, Mr.
Thomas Noddy, junior, is generally induced to
take up the case at the last moment, gratis,
without any evidence or any preparation or any
knowledge of the circumstances whatever ; and
in others the law now makes arrangements to
pay the same individual for his services at a
moderate rate, the other circumstances being
much the same as of old. In amazingly few
instances is there any c nee called for the
defence. The prisoner u unable to hunt up
evidence himself; he can seldom stand the
tremendous expense of hiring anyone to hunt
up evidence for him, the men whom the police
arrest on capital charges being all in exceed-
ingly low circumstances; and even where the
State allows the prisoner a certain sum for
his defence it only allows enough to hire a
cheap and muddled barrister, and leaves nothing
over to find materials for that barrister to
go upon. Consequently the defence consists
entirely on the “brilliant speech” of Mr. Noddy,
and as he knows practically nothing, and can
know nothing, of the matter except what he
has heard from the prosecution, his oration con-
sists partly of generalities and chiefly of wild
clawings and poundings at the empty air.
The various links of circumstantial evidence
may be all idiocy and hard lying, but this sort
of barrister is utterly ignorant of the fact, and
can oidy accept them as true, and try to explain
them away by asserting that his client is a
lunatic, or by vague whizzing noises concerning
Magna Charta, and the “ inalienable rights of a
British citizen,” and other matters quite as
remote and unavailing. The prisoner himself
may be equally ignorant, and equally unable to
explain matters. The cheap sixpenny knife
with blood and hair on the blade may be his or
it may not —the local ironmonger may have laid
in a dozen of the same pattern, and it may be
impossible to get at the truth without tracing
them all, for possibly it was the seventy-second
one of the lot with which the crime was com-
mitted. The prisoner’s boots may exactly fit
the footmarks on the high-road, on the strength
of which the case is built up, and considering
the absolute similarity of thousands of factory-
built boots, he may be utterly unable to tell
whether his boots actually caused these
marks or not unless he sees them. The
detectives have probably spent eight weeks’
hard labour and a large amount of
money in getting up their evidence, and
if the prisoner can hire an equal number of
detectives for a similar period he may be able
to find and produce another man whose boots
and whose knife fit the case just as well as his
do. In ninety-nine cases out of a hundred,
however, he is utterly unable to hire any
detective, and if he should by any chance be in
a position to do such a thing, there is nobody
whom he can hire except the useless and extor-
tionate private enquiry office fiend. And in the
hundredth case he is probably hanged when his
investigations are half through, because to
disprove the police evidence he has to begin his
proceedings where the police leave off. The
police may spend three months in hunting up
the one damning fact which will hang the
prisoner, but if he requires anything like the
same period, he will be comfortably hanged
before his business is half through.
* * *
Consequently he depends on the eloquence of
his cheap and hollow-sounding counsel, who
chiefly clears his throat because he has nothing else
to do, and who might as well lecture about the
crater of Vesuvius so far as any practical results
are concerned. Then the Judge sums up the
case and demolishes the wretched counsel’s
argument in one act. He also tells the
jury that the prisoner’s case has been care-
fully and ably represented, and, as a rule, the
jury utterly fails to see that the prisoner’s
case has never even been opened. And
so they bring in a verdict of guilty, and
the Bench solemnly informs the accused
that he has had an impartial hearing —
which is a hollow lie—and an equitable trial—
which is a glaring falsehood. And occasionally
it throws in a courteous allusion to the able
manner in which the evidence has been col-
lected for the prosecution. What becomes of
the police who don’t collect evidence in an
able manner, or at least in a sufficiently able
manner to hang any prisoner, nobody inquires,
but when the case of Mr. O’Callaghan is dis-
posed of a certain amount of light will probably
be thrown on this branch of the subject.
The “Society” Disease.
When account is taken of the many depart-
ments of human indulgence into which the iron
hand of legislation is sought to be thrust, it
comes as a surprise to find that there exists no
company of reformers proclaiming the necessity
of applying prohibitive or restrictive legislation
to the levees, “at homes,” the dinners, balls,
picnics and other swerves and swirls for which
Society, on its public and fashionable side,
displays so rapacious an appetite. When a man
with frayed sleeves, broken boots and a bottle
of beer appears in public many persons become
deeply affected. They connect the slatternly
state of his raiment with the liquor, and con-
stituting themselves an organisation command
Parliament to suppress public-houses. In-
dividuals are disposed to enjoy themselves on
Sundays, and to do so comfortably they require
the convenience of trams and boats. There is
no doubt that in popular enjoyments there is
an alloy of frivolity discordant to minds
screwed up to a severely-serious pitch of
solemnity, and here again on behalf of a shocked
sentiment an organisation creates itself and
marches to Parliament for legislation. There
is a society for putting down the use of
tobacco and one for putting down the use of
coffee. In fact, wherever it can be shown that
a habit, a practice, or an indulgence is followed
in the most remote way by evil, or apparent
evil, to the individual or the community, men
and women hand in their names, constitute
themselves an association, and proceed to urge
Parliament to frame laws prohibitive or restric-
tive of what they consider objectionable. That
is, men and women act and have acted thus in
regard to all matters except what we may call
the intoxication of fashionable life. We don’t
say that the associations referred to are to be
approved ; what we point to is the strange fact
that, with all this readiness to start crusades,
sometimes with the most trifling issues in view,
no crusaders are forthcoming as an organisation
or association, to direct their collective power
against society’s delirious extravagances.
# * *
On the ocean of alcoholism many thousands
drift. Sodden, soaked, they disappear, stupor
paralysing their brains, knowing nothing >f
whence they came or whither they go,
negations of reason and instinct. But on the
ocean ©f fashionable life, who will say
that the calamities are not greater? The
young and the old are there, with passions
mad as ever sprang from the wine-cup, purposes
more fell because more deliberately planned and
pursued, robbery is there, lust is there, murder
is there. The father is taught that it is his
duty to put wife and daughters to the front.
He may be one who finds little attraction for
himself in the garish throng ; but there is a
tyranny of circumstance to which he bows. To
the front they go, not because of intellectual or
moral worth, but because he is supposed to be
financially able to take his turn in keeping
society in feasts and pastimes. He may lie
financially able or he may not, but once in,
retreat without ignominy is almost impossible.
And if he be notable, then all the demons in the
abyss of man’s nature charge his ears with
whispered counsels. He may be one who can
turn his face about and march his family back
through an avenue of murdered hopes and >
trampled vanities, or he may be one who will
so strain his forces to sustain the .position, that
heart and brain will break and let him through
to a premature grave ; but if he be either, he
will be exceptional. Generally, once there he
will strive to stay.
# * *
Years may pass and such a man may still
stand in the front. As he grows feeble and his
eye dims, he may be pointed to as an example
of a fashionable life, which is also an honour-
able one. But what about the skeletons in
his cupboard? Had Bank-manager Bradley,
the last-tried Sydney defaulter, succeeded
in his rink speculations, would the world ever
know that only for peculations the speculations
would not have been born? His disgrace is
not a consequence of his having been a thief
and a forger, but in consequence of the public
having lost its taste for the amusement of
skating. Had the taste survived, or better
partnership management been employed,
Bradley would probably have posed as an ideal
entertainer to the end. The case is, we con-
tend, typical. The average man, with a family,
will be, owing to the false glory with which
“ society” is enveloped, easily persuaded to go
in ; and once in, the average man will not order
his wife and daughters to gather their skirts
about them and go out again while a chance of
holding ground is visible. He will straighten
his white tie with one hand, and with the other
feel if the phial of poison is safe in his inside
pocket. In his coffers is stolen gold. The
entertainment of the night will be paid for by
.the product of embezzlement, forgery, or the
malversation of trust-funds. If he is found out,
he has the poison or a prospect of escape by
flight.
* * #
How many prominent men in white ties
carry poison phials or loaded revolvers on
their persons, as Bradley says he did ?
How many start as they think they see
detectives in the shadows of their ball-
rooms, and cry out in their dreams as their
imaginations show them incriminating investi-
gations into their accounts going on ? The next
day these will be at the levee, the at-home, the
resort of pleasure, however, and so the swirl will
proceed until poison or shot or the gaol closes
the career tragically, or a turn of luck enables the
criminal to put back the plunder and find once
more the peace of an easy conscience. We have
called it surprising that, in a community where
crusaders can organise to put down cigarette-
smoking, there is no organisation to turn the
light of honest common sense on to the gigantic
engine of mischief which is. worshipped under
the name ofsociety.” No rock with a pirate
beacon blazing from its summit was ever so
remorseless a destroyer of the ships attracted
to it as is this rapacious monster of those who,
without the equipment of necessary wealth,
come within its influence. Through it wives are
corrupted, daughters dishonoured, and fathers
and sons driven from the peace of upright lives.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Philosophy and Foolery.
The fame of Tolstoi, which owes a good deal
to the modern Anglo-Saxon disposition to fossick
out strange idols, will scarcely be enlarged by
his latest deliverances on the great “ booze and
baccy ” question. The Russian novelist, being
himself a convert to the doctrine of non-
indulgence in everything that is nice, declares
“ it is not inclination, nor pleasure, nor distrac-
tion, nor amusement that gives us the universal
habit of consuming hashish, opium, wine and
tobacco, but the necessity for concealing from
oneself the records of one’s conscience.” This
may possibly explain the wherefore of a closing-
time push into a Moscow pub., or account for
the Russian sassiety ladies being so fond of
cigarettes on Sunday, but as a lesson for civilisa-
tion in general it is solemnly inane. When a
two-year-old Australian shrieks for a spoonful
of wine in his cold water, or exhibits a marked
preference for the milk that contains a nip
of brandy, he isn’t seeking to deaden his
memories of the flattened humming - top
that he took into the backyard and delibe-
rately thumped with a hammer. Neither
does a growing lad smoke brown paper behind
the fowlhouse in order that he may vomit up
his remorse for having played the wag. First
he smokes because it seems manly; presently
he smokes to air his accomplishment; next he
sucks at a pipe for the joy of getting it
coloured ; finally he becomes a human chimney,
because he derives real or imaginary pleasure
from his draws and puffs. Millions of poor
fellows daily allay their cravings for more
dinner with half a pipeful of tobacco, or a
melancholy chew. They have far less pain in
their consciences than in their stomachs.
Tolstoi’s ideas bear eloquent testimony to the
barbarism of a country whose ruler has to sleep
in a suit of armour, and can’t sit down sud-
denly for fear of exploding a mine. His mind
is provincial. What does the genius know of
this sunny land of ours, where the craftiest
swindles, as well ai the boldest daylight rob-
beries, are .committed by men who “ never
touch' anything ” which they can’t put into
their pockets ? Living out of the hurly-burly,
Tolstoi is manifestly ignorant of the fact
that the professional card-player “sharps” on
soda-water, and that the habit of total-
abstinence from tobacco and alcohol is quite the
ashion amongst those clear-skinned young mis-
creants who live upon the earnings of the demi-
monde. They, like lots of temperance
financiers, wash out the records of their con-
sciences with soft-tack. “A small squash, if
you please,” murmurs the pious land-boomer;
“ lemonade and mrsberry for me,” says Flash
Flo’s fancy-man. Strong drink makes us reck-
less, tobacco has a tendency to make us lazy
and dreamy. There are good men and true
who swear off' either indulgence from honourable
motives. Liquor also warms a naturally cold
heart until it becomes generous, and a calm pipe
creates compassion. There are bad men, d d
bad men, who take care to steer clear of these
“ vices,” in which virtues lie concealed.
Tolstoi, with a singular disregard for logic
in his reasoning, brackets together the
Eiffel Tower and obligatory military ser-
vices as equal illustrations of the world’s
squiffiness. He argues that the circumstance
of a company building the Parisian Tower, and
the further circumstance that millions of per-
sons have paid to get atop of it, proves that
everybody concerned must be blind drunk. He
doesn’t seem to understand that the financial
success of a bold scheme is convincing evidence
of the promoters’ sobriety. As for the illustra-
tion of compulsory military service —a state of
things which is patiently endured by the tem-
perate French, and mocked at by the bibulous
Britishers why, it merely proves that a
brilliant Russian writer of fiction can be a cast-
iron fool on questions of cosmopolitan fact.
Tolstoi has started to study human nature at
the wrong end. He has got to learn, no doubt,
that savage races never lose the desire to kill
and eat one another until they have tasted rum,
and no great naked nation has eve; been satis-
factorily civilised in the absence of vhat divine
spirit. Nevertheless, people smoke because it
is soothing and conducive to partial anaesthesia.
Therefore, Tolstoi’s “ conscience ” is probably
also lulled by “baccy;” but, it is not evident
that forgetfulness is an essential to the develop-
ment of immorality.
A Beastly Democrat.
Chief-Justice Lillev, of Queensland, lias ,
trodden on quite a number of ancient political
corns by attending a trades-union meeting, and
listening to the singing of the spirit-stirring
“ Marseillaise,” and when Parliament meets it
is expected that someone will “ call attention ”
to his conduct. What will happen after atten-
tion has been “ called ” is not known, but it is
vaguely assumed that it will be something
horrible, and that the Government will come
down with a roar and a ghastly crash on the
offender. Nominally, of course, a judge is not
in any sense responsible to Parliament for his
actions, so long as he discharges his duties
honourably and with reasonable intelligence,
and does not break all the ten commandments
at once, but in practice this freedom amounts
to very little. He has a sort of general license
to howl aloud for the cause of Property, and
when he does so the authorities invariably de-
cline to interfere on the ground that the bench
is not subject to political control; but should he
be suspected of an inclination to howl even in a
whisper for the cause of Labour then his sacred
character falls to pieces on the instant. The
charges against Sir Charles Lilley are three-
fold, and any one of the three is serious enough
to prove fatal. In the first place, he attended a
trades-union gathering, and though every poli-
tician in the Queensland Assembly regards
trades-unionism—when a general election is on
—as the noblest institution in the country, most
of them would as soon attend a snake, or a
quantity of condemned fish and guano, at any
other time. The men who propose to “ call
attention ” to the Chief Justice’s conduct have
almost all grovelled abjectly to the Unions and
the Churches and anything else that could get
them billets and votes, but they object to the
horny son of toil when there is nothing to be
gained by him, on account of his obvious want
of “ respectability,” and the bag of tools which
he carries on his back. Consequently, they feel
disgusted at a man who lias voluntarily associ-
ated himself with anybody or anything belong-
ing to Labour, just in the same way as the
fatted Archbishop shudders when he rubs
against one of his “ beloved brethren ” when
the “ brother ” is engaged in the honourable
avocation of carting dirt. In the second
place, Sir Charles Lilley listened without
protest to the singing of the “ Marseillaise, ”
and apparently the Government dislikes that
fiery hymn, and recognises that its denuncia-
tion of tyrants is a direct insult to itself
collectively and individually. Just here the
question naturally arises—supposing Judge
Lilley had attended a gathering of zoologists
who had met to consider the extinction of bulgy
reptiles, and the Government had suddenly
resolved that it was the reptiles alluded to—
what then ? The “ Marseillaise ” existed
before Queensland was heard of, and there is no
more reason why the Griffith Ministry should
boil over about that martial hymn than about
the voluminous curses of the Hebrew prophets.
And in the third place, the Chief Justice neg-
lected to get up on his hind legs and curse when
the meeting groaned at the Government. It
seems a gruesome suggestion to make, but,
perhaps, Sir Charles doesn’t like the Govern-
ment, and therefore simply let the meeting
groan. He couldn’t have prevented it groaning
anyhow, and he isn’t paid his salary for the
express end and purpose that he may admire
the Griffith Cabinet, and as he didn’t groan
himself he preserved a strictly impartial atti-
tude, so there doesn’t seem to be much to
complain of. But despite these precautions and
reservations on his part, Sir Charles Lilley
has got into a tight place. The charge against
him virtually is that by expressing his sym-
pathy with Labour he has demonstrated that he
cannot be trusted to adjudicate in any ques-
tions that may arise between Labour and
Capital—in other words, he may happen to
adjudicate the wrong way. He might have
dined at a bankers’ institute, or appeared at a
pastoralists’ banquet, or fraternised with a man
who wanted to “fire low” and demolish all
unionists, and the party of Property would
still have trusted him ; but instead of doing
these things, he appeared at a trades-union
gathering, and consequently the varnish of his
outer respectability has cracked.

A Husband’s Rights.
Ceorge Hoaee, a Daylesford (Yic.) jeweller,
has run away rather than “ face the scandal”
of prosecuting the jealous husband who took
corners oft him with a wood-axe. Little sym-
pathy is likely to be wasted upon this chipned
Lothario, in that he was giddy and they
found him out. Nevertheless,'' Hoare’s deter-
mination to hide his diminished head will rob
t he Law of a most desirable opportunity to lay
down rules for the better guidance of Othello,
as well as for the preservation of unscrupulous
lovers, be they jewellers, aides-de-camp, or—
humph 1 — even vice-regal personages. Ob-
viously, a husband, suddenly shocked by the
discovery that the wife he believed to be vir-
tuous has been quite otherwise, is not in such a
case accountable for what he does to the other
fellow, if the other fellow be within arm’s
leach. An uncontrollable impulse compels him
to strike, with or without a weapon, at the
intruder—always provided that the intruder
hasnt already committed further immorality
by knocking the avenger down. The punish-
ment of instantaneous death has ere now
been meted out to the man engaged in
coveting his neighbour’s wife when his
neighbour came home unexpectedly. And
the Law has maintained the justice of such
slaughter, even though the outraged husband
may himself have been a less excusable adul-
terer than the person he slew. The Law allows
us to get carried away by certain sacred “feel-
ings ” in the moment of those feelings being
really, truly, and genuinely shocked. But
jealousy, even the most legalised jealousy, does
not entitle a husband to commit a cold-blooded
murder, nor to “ wait upon ’ the man who has
wronged him, with reckless indifference to what
may happen when they meet. The Daylesford
husband, one Thomas Avison, was not startled
out of his senses when he found the jeweller
usiu ping bis position. On the contrary, he
slunk into his home by the back door expecting,
as he told the friend whom be so prudently
asked to accompany him, that he would find
his wife in the jeweller’s arms. It is not
unfair to say that this irate husband had set a
trap for the man who, when caught in a trap,
he proceeded to chop about with a tomahawk.’
As tomahawks are not usually picked up in
bedrooms we may suppose that Mr. Avison,
like a thoughtful Othello, took his instrument
of destruction into the room with him. In
short, he deliberately laid himself out to maltreat
the defenceless man who had robbed him of his
wife’s affection—if he ever had it. The good
folks of Daylesford are understood to be loud
in their praises of Avison, and their denuncia-
tions of the wicked jeweller, but had the latter
brought his wounds into Court he might at
least have enjoyed the satisfaction of hearing
the outraged husband told a few wholesome
truths, and perhaps sentenced to three months.
Avison may think himself lucky that the other
fellow hadn’t pluck to pay back blow for blow,
and the guilty female is to be sympathised with
m her choice of a lover who was even less of a
man than her husband.
Another intelligible item about the Queensland
shearers, from a monopolistic daily paper .
A few more arrests have been made. Evidence of a
violent programme is accumulating. The township is
(jiuefc. Considerable drunkenness is noticeable, but no
disorder.
There is a lot of information in that paragraph
when you come to look at it. Evidence of
violence and wild insurrection is accumulating,
And there is “no disorder.” Bloodshed and
carnage arc impending, but “the township is
quiet.” In a general sort of wav a terrible
calm is raging, and the insurgents are filling the
air with fearful blasts of profound silence. Also
the men have suddenly continued to sit down
and not say anything in a way that justifies the
worst forebodings, and the manner in which
they are doing nothing in particular wants to
be put down, lest they should grow reckless
and do it again. Furthermore, they have been
seen sleeping in an ominous fashion which has
caused the authorities to telegraph for rein-
forcements, and the footprints of the hand of
anarchy are visible in the deep and darkly sus-
picious way that nothing has been heard for a
ong time about the insurrection which hasn’t
taken place.

Sydney Echo, while admitting that Sir George
t*REY s speech in favour of making the
Governor-Generalship of the Commonwealth
was earnest,” says “it was hardly taken
seriously. So far as that goes, we have never
yet met anyone who took the Echo seriously
unless it was one of the Messrs. Fairfax as he
looked lugubriously at the monthly balance-
sheet. I hat, however, is by the way. Few
motions of reform have been taken seriously
when first promulgated, yet that circumstance
lias not in any way affected the ultimate con-
quest of the race. Mirabeau was taken any-
img but seriously in the States-General, but
the guillotine was not a joke by any means to
those people whose heads tumbled into the
basket. Not that we compare Grey with
Mirabeau, except in this respect; Both were
men who, born aristocrats, learned, in their

extend the power of the Common People.
One of the effects of the traitorous act by which
the federal Convention referred the franchise
of the new Parliament to the States for settle-
ment will be the absolute annihilation of the
vole of the itinerant labourer. Had the Con-
stitution been erected on manhood suffrage it
would only have been a matter of time—per-
haps the thing would have been done in the first
session of the Federal Parliament—for residence
m any one electoral district of one State to have
been made a qualification for voting in that
district, even though the elector was nominally
claimed as a unit of some other State Thus
a Victorian or New South Wales shearer
who goes to the North on shearing tour and
works southwards will almost certainly be dis-
qualified year by year, simply because he is
not sufficiently long a resident in any one
electoral district to qualify. As with shearers
so with thousands of railway navvies, seamen
and the other forms of itinerant labour The
organisation of the vote of nomadic labour is
one of the most pressing needs of the hour.

Someone writes to Sydney D.T., quite
surprised that a water-bottle, refracting the sun’s
rays, should act as a burning-glass. Heaps of
fences and miles of grass are destroyed annually
through flames raised by the sun in conjunction
with carelessly-thrown-down bottles. Fires have
of ten been purposely kindled by such means, and
tall lies are told about the cooking of fat steaks
by the suns rays focussed on a nicely-adjusted
sheet of glass.

An apparently inspired paragraph from
Sydney D.T.
The Convention delegates insisted on building upon
lines against which every instinct of Sir Henry Parkes’
political nature revolts, consequently he sat with folded
arms nearly all the time.
What does this mean ? Does it not mean that the
slippery Parkes, should it suit him, will stump
the country against his own Constitooshun ?
During the live weeks of the Convention
the Union Jack floated over N.S.W. Parliament
buildings. The other day someone mentioned to
Parkes that the fact had been commented upon—■
(by The Bulletin, which enquired why the flag
should be run up over the Convention when it did
not float over the ordinary sessions of Parliament
and suggested that it was indicative of Parkes’
special loyal effort). Parkes said, “ why Hi
didn’t know the flag was there !” ’
Sir George Grey hammered away so con-
sistently at great democratic principles in the
Convention that four-fifths of the delegates re-
garded him as a bore. Parkes smiled pityingly
at him and winked his eve at other delegates
as though to say, “What an old flapdoodler
“ ,8 » while Cdhes persistently sneered at
the old Pro-Consul. “ The hon. member mis-
understands me,” said Sir George, gently, to the
Hon. Duncan Gillies one afternoon. “I never
have understood the hon. member,” returned
Gillies with a derisive laugh. Grey looked
at him for a second or two, and then said in his
usual slow and smooth style : “I don't misunder-
stand the hon. member; I fathom him.” Gillies
went under.

Taken altogether, the N.S.W. delegates
showed up badly during the Federal Convention.
The better the delegates became acquainted with
1 arkes, the more contempt they had for his pro-
fessed ability; M‘Millan was a frivolous fizzle •
Hibbs was a bunch of Chinese crackers, always
popping and spluttering in a weak sort of way •
Abbott was a frost, except in the chair, where he
made the most of his square jaw and iron hand •
Jennings was neither useful nor ornamental ; and
buttor was only of service as Parkes’ man Friday.
Parkes would say : “ Hoes hany gentleman second
this? and look straight at Suttor. Suttor had
perhaps been dozing, until he felt the Eve upon
him, when he would straightway arise from his
trance and second the motion. The delegates
who did most work were those from South Aus-
tralia and Tasmania, with Queensland, Victoria
Maonland, N.S. Wales, and W.A. following in
the order given. Griffith, of Queensland, per-
formed most of the labour, and little Clark of
Tasmania, was close up to him. The man who
wanted to put in a big innings at democratic work
winch should make the day of Australian union
worth celebrating in the future was not permitted
to have a finger in the Constitutional pie, but his
persistency has awakened Australia to the imme-
diate necessity of a strenuous fight for equal
voting and equal justice. This was Old Man Grey.

“Schoolmistress,” at Sydney Criterion, is
a vastly-improved show. Maggie Moore, the
instructress, who vows, at tire conclusion, to keep
her loafing spouse on low Di/ott, has toned down,
and the others have tinted up; the result is—
harmony. Miss Romer’s timid, faltering, flustered
female is an excellent impersonation. Mr.
Clitherow’s excellent little character sketch of
Otto Bernstein is reminiscent of Baron Von
Mueller. Pattie Browne, at times, so burlesques
the romantic school that she suggests Brown-
Potter, and Dot Boucicault’s “pompous pauper”
is much less Parisian cafe and boulevardian than
on first night. Percy Lyndal looks more of a man
and less of a curate when he wears a beard, and
we should advise him always to don one when “on
the mash.” The after-dinner speech of George
Anson’s apoplectic and irascible Admiral is a very
happy one, and his indictment of the humbug,
Queckett, is even better, though a regular Chinese
puzzle in a logical way. The classical contingent,
with Mr. and Mrs. 8., George Titheradge, Cecil
Ward (they concluded the Brisbane season with
1 Modern Wives,”) open on Friday night, as
previously announced, in “ The Colonel.”
» * » jfc
At Sydney Garrick business has been
good, and the “Widow, Esq.,” has drawn some
illigant dress-circles. .Johnny Sheridan’s new
song, “ The Shwate Kilkenny Band,” is likely to
become very popular. One weak point about the
show is a tendency to withhold all the best people
from the stage in the lump. Johnny skips to
Melbourne Royal on the 24th, and Myra Kemble
opens for a two weeks’ season of “Dr. Bill” or
“My Flat ”on Saturday week. Janet Achurch
follows on Oth May, probably with “Masks and
Faces. ”
* * * *-
At Sydney Her Majesty’s, that lugubrious
and lackadaiscal seaman, “My Jack,” we arc
“jack” about him, gets more support than his
personal merit entitles him to. The blind lady of
Mrs. Rignold is a harmonious, clean-cut and
dignified piece of work, and Jack Sweeney’s Irish
tar furnishes the only glimpse of sunshine the
tearful tale can boast of. The “ Corsican
Brothers ” will be put up on Saturday.

That series of extraordinary inslances of
the special interposition of Providence, named
ihe Golden Ladder,’ wJI soon be rung down
(in this connection the word “rung” is appro-
priate) at Sydney Royal, and the Sims-Pettit
play “ London, Day by Day ” will be unfolded for
the first time to a Sydney audience on Saturday.
Business at the old house has been highly payable
throughout the season.
** * *
At Sydney Natatorium, last Saturday
afternoon, W. J. Gormley, of the Enterprise
Club son, we believe, of the member for Wagga
—with six watches on him, swam 100yds. in Imin.
and 200yds. in 2min. 25 l-osec., thus beating
the English amateur record by a trifle less than ssec.
Gormley, who makes as much wash as a Balmain
ferry-boat, was too much exhausted to go on in
pursuit of the 220yds. record. However, he’ll
annex that at a later date.
*- * * *
At Sydney School of Arts, Fred. Garnett’s
Silence and Fun ” act is a big feature. The
little man has put on flesh of late years, but is as
nimble and boneless as ever. The glove-fight
between Tom Thumb and Frank Finger, two
sturdy dwarfs, is comical. A new corner-man,
Tommy Rosman, has been engaged, and “the
sensational song and dance ladies, the Sisters
Anderson, have been engaged at an “enormous
salary.” More thirty-pounders? Professor
Rhodes presides at the piano.
** * *
At Coogee Aquarium the Sunday concerts
are being kept up to date by the engagement of
Nina Osborne, Fred. Spencer, Miss Swift, the
siffleuse, and other good people.
*m * *
The sword-contest at Bondi Aquarium,
between Ma’mselle Hermione and Professor Par-
ker, was won by the lady, after a lengthy tussle
and some disputes over the deciding hit, by a
point. The sympathies of the crowd were, of
course, with the representative of the softer sex.
** * *
Had the programme of the preliminary
Albu concerts been selected on similar lines to
the third, it is more than probable that these
talented young ladies would not have had quite
such a large audience as that which greeted them
on their first Town Hall appearance, last Thurs-
day. Not only do Sydney music-lovers like
grand arias, but, in addition to the taste culti-
vated by many admirable local ballad-singers,
they have had many opportunities, from the days
of Catherine Hayes down to those of Charles
Santley and Janet Whytock Patey, of discovering
that simplicity, clearness of enunciation and
feeling” are indispensable for ballad-singing.
Now the forte of the Misses Albu, particularly
Julie, does not lie in that direction. In fact, we
think that the theatrical straining for effect in
“Love’s old, sweet Song,” which broke the time
and destroyed the significance of that delightful
melody, furnished an admirable example of how
ballads should not be sung. This (Thursday)
evening concludes the present season, when the
sisters will sing a duet, and will have more suit-
able matter for the display of their fine, highly-
trained voices than on the last occasion,.

Arthur Gordon has transferred his Sunday
concerts to the Me-Mel, and Harry Rickards is
utilising the interval prior to his departure by
running the Alathea on the same lines, twice on
that day.

SPECIAL AWARDS
To the value of about £2OO have been offered to the com-
mittee of management by leading business people in Mel-
bourne, particulars of which will be shortly announced.
TO THE LADIES.
Ladies wishing to exhibit their children must, at time
of entry, clearly fill in the form provided for that pur-
pose, and which may be obtained at the Office, Exhibi-
tion Building- (Nicholson-street entrance).
Entries mnst be made, with the Secretary if possible,
prior to 5 p.m. on the 22nd April. For the convenience
of parents residing- in the country or in adjoining
colonies, entry-forms will be posted free on application.
The Committee have engaged a Special Staff of Trained
Nurses, who will see that every attention and care is
bestowed upon the children and their parents, guardians
or nurses during the Exhibition.
Certificate of registration of birth must be produced to
the judges.
The judging in the several classes will be controlled by
a committee consisting of prominent medical gentlemen.
The awards for the Champion Prizes and Special Prizes
will be made by public vote. Every person paying for
admission to the Exhibition Building will receive a
ballot-paper, upon which they can vote for the children
they consider entitled thereto. This ballot system will
be controlled by a committee, to be elected by the exhi-
bitors themselves.
The committee, which consists of prominent medical
specialists, intend giving lectures every afternoon and
evening to mothers on “Nursing and the General Treat-
ment of Children,” and will distribute a pamphlet for the
guidance of parents,
The limited number of entries are rapidly being
received, and early application is necessary.
On behalf of the Committee of Management,
T. G. HAMPTON, Secretary.
beauty to stand around and smile at his eccentri-
cities and a ballet which, for delirious abandon
and poetry of kick, surpasses all previous efforts
on the Princess’ stage. Its exciting whirl causes
big beads of perspiration to sparkle upon every
manly brow in the house. Its effect upon the
female organisation must be left for the pens of
lady reporters to portray. With Elton laid up,
and the ballet all down with lumbago, there would
be little “Old Guard” left to talk about, however.
It is not an attractive musical work. Certainly
not as taking as the same composer’s “Paul
Jones,” which is poor again by comparison with
his “ Cloches de Corneville.” There are no grand
finales, and no anything in the way of melody
which doesn’t immediately recall a brighter tune
to recollection. But as a farcical military
comedy, written for one character and extrava-
gantly interpreted by the right person, “The Old
Guard ”is distinctly good. The scene is laid m
Napoleonic times, and the story deals with the
gory Corsican’s decree that one of his many
captains shall marry the daughter of a marquis of
the old noblesse. The Marquis, wishing to avoid
such a catastrophe to the blood of his ancestors,
allows his daughter to change places with the
supposed base-born offspring of Elton, a vulgar
village mayor person who maintains his official
position on a business of 16 barrels per week. In
brief, Elton keeps a pub., where the local popula-
tion are expected to get drunk when they are not
singing choruses. Sometimes they sneak away
and ‘ 1 set ’em up ” at a ri val pub. round the corner,
a dereliction of duty that disgusts the mayor
beyond measure. But this by the way. In due
course Elton learns that the two daughters were
mixed up soon after birth, consequently the
ignoble captain is really going to marry the right
girl instead of the wrong girl, as was intended by
the Marquee. Each, girl, however, puts matters
right by falling in love with different fellows who,
for their parts, turn out to be one another, and so
everybody marries somebody in a suitable
“sphere of life.” The Marquee clasps his in-
-1 tended son-in-law to his coat-of-arms without
much family pride, whilst the worthy licensed
victualler escapes the ignominy of becoming
related by marriage to the naughty old nobility
of France. Some small amount of interest
attaches to the musical love affairs of the two
young couples, and the Marquee is not quite a
nonentity. Elton is fully three-quarters of the
plot, though. He has never worked harder with
his head, legs, or hands, and some of his acrobatic
business with a musket is an entertainment in
itself. He takes such complete possession of the
opera that one cannot imagine it ever being suc-
cessfully performed (on this side of the line) with-
out his assistance. Ida Osborne (she, like Flora
Graupner, improves in the art of dressing)
is a vocal success; likewise Florence Young,
who begins to look happy and to show a
considerable intelligence on the stage. Ryley’s
dreadful way of combing his hair is the only thing
against him in the monotonous part of the Captain,
but Leumane is less satisfactory in a feebler char-
acter. Howard Yernon is always at home as the
noble fellow conspirator of the little mayor,
although he unfortunately happened tobea liUe
hoarse (original joke!) on Saturday. Vio.ut
Varley, with a pleasant song, and Flora Graup-
ner, without a note, chip in occasionally along
with the Old Guard which gives the opera an un-
suitable name.
** * *
“Falka,” at the Opera House, loses “go”
as time progresses. Lay Brother Pelican, in the
hands of Knight Aston, is found to be a growing
affliction, also such trifles as Lena Salinger’s
smile are giving some cause for complaint. All
things considered, the setting up of the other
opera will surely necessitate an early withdrawal
of “ Falka,” in which nothing, except Snazelle’s
cultivated -warble, seems to improve on acquaint-
ance. Miss Merivale has novelties in store, they
say, and the dress-circle is evidently waiting for
one to be fetched out.
** * *
Business in the more expensive parts of
Melbourne Bijou took a sudden spurt with the
production of “ Doll’s House ” on Wednesday of
last week. Sassiety came along and yawned in
such considerable quantities that Ibsen’s play was
expected to pay its way for eight nights instead
of three, and all idea of producing “ The Wager ”
was joyfully abandoned. By Saturday, how-
ever, the demand for petty details, spiced with
strange philosophy, had collapsed, and Miss
Achurch jumped about before one of the thinnest
houses of a poor season. Probably the public
have lost more interest in the actress than in
Ibsen. Her style, always hard, has become
harder during her residence in Australia, and her
oddities have no longer the charm of novelty to
recommend them. The characters of Nora and
Torvald Helmer, as played by Miss Achurch and
Charles Charrington, are placed in impossible
positions. No such snuffling prig as Torvald
could monopolise the heart and soul of Ibsen’s
Nora for five years, or five minutes. A man of
his sort —the frock-coated, white-livered, plati-
tudinising pillar of commerce and dull “ respecta-
bility ’’—never enjoys the love of any woman save
the mother who suckled him, and even mother
prefers his wicked younger brother, who is reck-
less, amusing, and a general disgrace to the
family. Neither could Miss Aehurch’s Nora play
at being a “little songbird,” and scream for
maecaroons, previous to the arrival of that mys-
terious miracle. “New Magdalen ” goes up on
Wednesday for two nights, and on Friday the
star benefits in “ Forget-Me-Not.” A managerial
benefit is not an event to gush over, however.
** * *
The regular Bijou followers will roll up
again on Saturday to welcome some of the old
B. and B. faces in “The Schoolmistress,” and
clap the two paws of good fellowship together in
honour of Maggie Moore.
** * *
‘‘Belphegor, the Mountebank,” is well
suited to an Alexandra audience, who like to take
their pathos in a plain, straightforward way, and
want nothing left to the imagination. Dampier s
performance, which runs on Charles Dillon lines,
even to the make-up, kept the boys very good on
Saturday night, and fetched a big burst of
applause at the end of the second act. Miss Lily,
too, and Mabel Russell (as the Henri who is
balanced on the back of a chair) played their best,
and the whole show was put through with suffi-
cient feeling to satisfy the not yet extinct craving
for a weep. It will be a pity when there are no
more people found willing to enter into the simple
joys and sorrows of Belphegor and his family, and
derive homely comfort from the thought that it
all came right in the end. Mr. Dampier starts
his “Shakespearean Fridays” this week with “As
You Like It.” On Saturday afternoon he gives
the use of the big house for a matinee-benefit to
the widow and orphans of Mr. Fewster, an Akgus
compositor, who was recently killed on the rail-
way-line. . And in the evening he revives “The
Wages of Sin,” which in real life are usually £5OOO
a year and a periodical trip to Yurrup.
** * *
“Moths” leave the Royal after Friday,
and may anon be seen, gnawing away at the ermine
of sassiety on the Princess’ stage. One last word
of praise may be given to Miss Nethersole’s
infinite tenderness in the last act, her interviews
with Lord Jura and the ’Murrikan duchess being
each of them two-column subjects, and worthy of
a poem. There is only room for the poem—
After this artistic production—George Darrell.
He is going to “star”—for George always does
star until we wonder what we are —for a grand
farewell fortnight, and the trouble starts on Satur-
day with “ Transported for Life.” They say he
intends to transport himself to England. But not
for life. No such bad luck.
** * *
All the other shows are carrying on in an
average manner —Gaiety, Victoria, Waxworks,
Cyclorama, and the Aquarium Alligator. The
People’s Palace has left off fetching the ungodly,
because there is a limit to the fascinations of even
a Punch and Judy Show. But the church people
are still trickling in at a low figure, and as the
expenses are said to have been covered all the
money now paid at the turnstiles is understood to
go straight to the good cause. On April 29th.
another Baby Show opens at the Exhibition.
Babies, it seems, are fatter and more plentiful
than ever. Candidates will be accepted, too, up
to the age of 30 months, so when prizes come to
THE MISSES ALBU.
be distributed we may expect to see a herd of
disappointed infants walking straight up to the
committee and giving them beans.
** * *
Alec. Mayne, business manager at the
Bijou, is just off for a trip “ home,” with one eye
on his health and the other looking after B. & B.
interests.
** * *
Despite the fact that indisposition on two
occasions withdrew the leading lady from the bill,
“Dr. Bill” was a big success throughout his two
weeks’ season at Adelaide Royal. He has now
finally taken in his brass-plate and “Our Flat,”
produced too late for us to discuss its merit, or
otherwise, this week, reigns in its stead. “Our
Flat ” is a three-act farcical comedy by Mrs.
Musgrove. Williamson and Garner hold the
Australian rights, and it has not been played in
this country before. At Garner’s Rooms, Jack
Cameron has a good minstrel company in full
swing; the Cycloraraa flourishes, while Scotch
Kennedy has been giving an entertainment in the
Town Hall.
** * *
On the 29th of this month the Federal
Baby-Show opens at Melbourne Exhibition,
and will continue to suck its thumb and
squall until the 2nd prox. Many ladies and
gentlemen who have achieved heaven-born infants
weighing two tons and under have already entered
their exhibits, and those who have not are re-
quested to hurry up if they want any floorage
space. The chief perpetrators of the entertain-
ment are Messrs. Arons and Glass, and people are
advised to camp in Nicholson-street early next
week and so avoid the inevitable crush.

Theatre.

Old Charlie Wheatleigh, who brought the “ Colleen
Bawn’’ and “Shaughraun” to Australia, is Sir Peter
Teazle, in Augustin Daly’s New York revival “School for
Scandal.” Harry Edwards is the Sir Oliver, Ada Rehan,
the Lady Teazle. Wheatleigh fairly replaces the eminent
Gilbert.
The success of Sullivan’s “ Ivanhoe ” causes revival of
Andrew Halliday’s, in London. Twenty years ago, at
Drury, under Chatterton, the run was on Halliday’s
“Ivanhoe,” “Amy Robsart,” “Talisman,” &c., as
spectacles, in which figured Adelaide Neilson, Creswick,
and Anderson.
Melbourne Royal management restores the good old pit,
gradually encroached on by stalls, till they ate it up, and
the exasperated pittites were hoisted to that infernal den,
the gallery'. Wherefore they couldn’t be garnered in, by
the divine Billiamson, and the gallery was given up to the
brutes who spit all night.
Rev. G. Rowe, a smart Adelaide parson with literary
tastes, did a clever thing the other day. Mark Guy
Pearse, the English lecturer, was ill, and could not fulfil
his Moonta (S. A.) engagement. Rowe learnt off the lec-
ture at a few hours’ notice, and delivered it with great
success.
Patey, the solicitor’s clerk, against whom something
is charged in connection with the Bijou fires, is an old-
established dabbler in Melbourne theatrical publications.
His latest “literary” venture was a short-lived sheet
called The Live Bird, which turned out an uncommonly
bad egg.
A New York ballet-girl dances in the same row as her
mother and grandmother. The old lady can still kick
a good six inches higher than either of her descendants.
Which goes to prove that legs are not what they used
to be.

Signor Majeroni explains that the Marquis Camillo del
Grille, whose death is reported, was not the husband of
Adelaide Ristori. Ristori and her husband, Juliano, now
reside at Rome. The Majeronis, those admirable artistes
and cultured, kindly people, everyone will regret to know,
are still in delicate health. As a small token of sympathy
it has been decided to give them a benefit, and a pre-
liminary meeting has been held at Her Majesty’s.
