Ambassador Lytton has entertained Madame Melba at
a banquet in Paris. Lytton, it may be remembered, was
the giddy Governor of India who struck a medal in honour
of the talents of a fascinating lady artiste, then in the

Barnum is dead, aged 84. He started life as a showman
by exhibiting “ George Washington’s old nurse,” a
coloured woman, aged 161, so he said ; and steadily
worked up from that comparatively humble starting-point
to a plane of mendacity which will alike be the envy
and the despair of all future generations of liars.
Barnum was very pious and travelled on the game. This
world is the oyster of the arch-liar who can become a
churchwarden.
Sydney Star’s cable padder-out has been describing a
fire in Mine. Melba’s apartments and her escape by a
ladder. The cabled skeleton the Star’s ungram-
matical inkslinger expanded into three-fourths of a
column. He concludes by stating that her rescuer—
“ With the greatest difficulty and danger gained the
window, smashed in the panes with his clenched fist, and
discovered the diva quite stupefied with the smoke-laden
atmosphere of the apartment. Gripping her firmly in his
arms, he carried her towards the shattered window, robed
only in her nightdress he once more gained the steps of
the ladder, which he successfully "descended with his
precious burden. The morning was bitterly frosty, and
in the transit, almost deshabille, Madame Melba caught a
severe cold, and will be prevented from singing for some
time.” Why the gentleman should have thought it necessary
to don Melba’s nightgown (the incident has a “ Sonnani-
bula ” flavour) ere descending the ladder, is not
evident’ nor is it apparent why the deshabille of “the
transit” should have given Melba cold, but the
deshabille of this cable-distorter is likely to give the
“ Star ” a bad cold shortly.
A Melbourne paper, enumerating all the innocuous
glories of the “People’s Palace,” mentioned that the
management had “even provided a shadow pantomime.”
Which somehow reminds us of the Daily Telegraph re-
porter who once summarised the entertainment at a
chapel festival as “an endless variety of instruction and
amusement, including buns and lemonade.”
We touched up the abolition of the grand old man from
the modern stage—the character enacted in London by
Farren and Chippendale, in America by Blake and Gil-
bert, in Australia by Rogers and Lambert. It remains to
sigh over the departure of the old woman, -the Mrs.
Phillips, Mrs. Wooldridge, and Mrs. Jones.
G. V. Brooke’s Sydney residence having been located, it
may be added that his Melbourne house was in Flinders-
lane, on the east side of the corner now occupied by Dr.
Rowan as his private hospital. The Mrs. G. V. Brooke of
the establishment was a mysterious lady, not Madame
Marie Duret. Brooke afterwards married Avonia Jones.
He was not an intellectual man off the stage, though men
like Jas. Smith, Blair, Sinnett, and the luminaria of the
era used to do their best to draw him out. Yet he
struggled manfully against the Bohemian tendencies he
had in common with Edmund Kean, and of him it might
be said, as Charles Lamb said of another actor: “ Every-
body liked him for his no pretensions at all.” Never for-
gotten.
Dear Bulletin, —“Poverty Point” declares re John
Knipe, of Melbourne, that he has never been an actor.
Apropos , let me tell you a yarn. When John first
landed in Victoria, after many vicissitudes he Joined a
Co. of travelling players at Bendigo diggings. They had
very bad luck, and at last the ghost ceased to walk at all.
The Co. consulted together, and finally' agreed each to
take a benefit commencing with the “ first lady ” and
descending the scale, each to choose the to be
played on his or her behalf. When it came to Knipe’s
turn (he was about the last, being in low’est ranks of the
players)he chose “ Jack Sheppard,” a piece which at that
time was not permitted to be played on the diggings with-
out special consent. The Co. said it was impossible to
have this piece, but Knipe was determined, and rode
away to a prominent magistrate and persuaded him to
give permission. The piece was played, and, of course,
was a tremendous success. John Knipe cleared nearly a
hundred pounds, which was the commencement of his
present fortune.—Yours, Jellyfish.

superior virtue.

as (air, “ Ben Bolt ”)

House. He brings the nucleus of a company—twelve
in all—with him, including Agnes Paul, Edith Cole,

General Sherman was a bit outspoken. “He called
into the Roosevelt’s one night late, when the curled male
darling who sat at the piano said, with a lisp: ‘What
kind of music do you like, General —a morceau of Chopin,
something by Mozart, or a wee bit of Beethoven?’ ‘ Any
kind except piano music,’ said the General, carelessly,
after surveying the musician for a moment. Then Miss

hear you sing, Blanche, dear. You know' your voice
always soothes him.’ ‘ Excuse me, madam, but I don’t

Only one church on this globe has a ballet. It is
Seville Cathedral, w'here, once a year, on Corpus Christi
Sunday, a religious ballet is danced in front of the altar,
but only by male dancers. It dates from time imme-
morial, and is regarded as a solemn function.

gress -indeed, never wwote anything since so good as the
“ Lights,” wherefore Archer contrasts his stagnation with
the progress of Jones. The question is whether Jones or
Pinero will fly the highest.

WATSON’S
EYE
LOTION
Tales of Old Sydney.
No. IV.—Mr. Pounce’s Reprieve.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Do you recollect how Copper-Plate
Josephs, an experienced bank-note forger, estab-
as a card-plate engraver in Jamieson-street,
rp- S y dne y> received a commission from Mr.
Timothy Pounce, the expert in concocting pardons
and tickets-of-leave, to engrave his (Pounce’s)
name on his card-ease ? If you do, you will also
remember how “ Copper-Plate,” just by way of a
joke, you know, took the trouble to print a few
cards like this :
Timothy Pounce,
Writer and Forger,
Macquarie-street.
Documents of all kinds
forged and uttered. Pardons
a speciality.
And to mix them up with the pretty scripts with
which Mr. Pounce was accustomed to introduce
himself and his professed business to the patron-
age of the good people of the old town.
Here is a copy of Mr. Pounce’s proper card: —
Timothy Pounce,
Writing Clerk,
Macquarie-street.
Documents of all kinds
drafted and written. Legal
work a speciality.
In this narrative we have to recount what
happened when Mr. Pounce handed one of the
former style of pasteboards to Captain Charles
Sturt, H.B.M.’s 30tli Regiment, by mistake for
one of the others.
* * *
One day Mr. Pounce, having taken a
morning constitutional on the racecourse, was
returning home to his bower of domesticity—he
lived in a little cottage in Macquarie-street built
on the site occupied at a later date by that parti-
cular house in Lyons’ Terrace which was tenanted
for some years by Judge Alfred Stephen (vou can
see “Stephen” scratched in half-a-dozen places
on the window-panes now) when he chanced to
espy a military-looking figure standing just
where William-street takes the sudden dip to
Woolloomooloo.
“ Captain Sturt, for a dollar!” ejaculated Mr.
Pounce. “He’ll be wanting some one, I’ll swear,
to assist him with his journals for the London
press.” And impelled by the thought that in the
brilliant galaxy of rogues which Sydney at this
time could furnish he was the one competent man
to aid the great Explorer in the preparation of his
book for publication, he strode towards the Cap-
tain. Very anxious was Mr. Pounce to get into
confidential intimacy with all men in high places.
There were so many little perquisites which
attached to Officialdom, and Mr. Pounce, keen
man of the world as he was, knew perfectly well
that the indirect method—such as a grant of land,
or a fat contract, or a dozen blank pardon-forms,
or a casual “ minute ” dropped promiscuously in a
mass of documentary matter—by which an official
would pay for “confidential services,” was oftener
more valuable than direct remuneration. That
was one reason why Mr. Pounce cultivated the
big men of the colony. And another was one that
’ Mr. Pounce hardly dared whisper to himself.
For, you see, Mr. Pounce’s intimacy with an
official generally meant a perfect mastery by Mr.
Pounce of the official's handwriting. And we
scarcely need tell you how useful that capacity
could be made in Old Sydney days by a gentle-
man like Pounce, who was as expert with the pen
as he was facile of conscience.
“Ever hear of Old Pounce, the forger?” we
once asked a solicitor’s managing-clerk, who had
held his post for over forty years in a Sydney
office.
“Old Pounce? God bless my soul, sir, when
any property comes into our hands to prove title,
and I see that the deeds bear what purport to be
’s, and ’s, and ’s signatures, I
am never at rest till I get ’em under Torrens’
title. That’s what I know of Old Pounce, as you
call him. His expertness has made us doubt even
documents dated at a time when he was out of the
colony under sentence of transportation to. Van
Dieman’s Land or Norfolk Island.”
That was Old Pounce.
* # *
A genial, pleasant-looking person he looked
in his blue coat and white ducks, and frilled shirt,
and snowy hair, as he strode swiftly and erectly
across the treeless expanse which now sustains the
umbrageous glory of Hyde Park. Well up in
years was Friend Timothy, but as alert and as
strong on his legs as a boy of twenty. As he
neared Captain Sturt, for the figure was the
Captain’s, he slowed his pace. So great and
dignified a man—Pounce did not think much of
him as a discoverer, what he honoured in Sturt
was his Brigade-Majorship—was to be approached
with deference.
The Captain, from the hilly eminence where he
stood, was gazing at a party of convicts cutting
down the William-street extension. His thoughts
were not, however, on the scene, prolific of con-
trasts, which wooed the light of his steel-grey
eyes. Over the dusty, jangling wretches that
toiled laboriously with pick and shovel; over the
overseers, who, to prevent themselves from falling
asleep in the sunny balminess, as well as to keep
their proteges up to the desired point of assiduity,
flicked the short-handled, long-thonged whips
here on to a face that had once been human, and
there on to shoulders that had once been brawny ;
over the soldiers of the guard as they leaned on
their shotted muskets; over the sparse Woolloo-
mooloo buildings ; over to the bold brown of the
distant headlands as they jutted into the sapphire
of the harbour; over all, glanced and roved
Captain Charlie’s vision, and yet he saw them not.
He was looking into the future with the prescient
orb of the man who is at once imaginative and
practical, and speculating, as was his constant
habit, over the possibilities with which the time
was pregnant. Darling had called him a dreamer,
and John Mackanass, after he had been deposed
from the post of Sheriff for making remarks
offensive to Church and State,” spoke of him as
a visionary. The men who were at his right hand
during his expeditions, Hamilton Hume and
George Macleay, used to say the same of him,
with an addition—that he dreamt dreams, and
saw visions, but he knew how to make them real.
Sturt saw in fancy the waving field of corn where
he found aridity, and white sails gleaming over
inland waters which he had found trackless;
and he did his best —a best which ranks with
Livingstone’s, and beside whicli Stanley’s is an
mfamy— to sow the corn and to spread the sail,
the men who rubbed shoulders with him in Old
Sydney before and after his expeditions thought
he was cracked when his thin lips quivered and
his steely eyes gave forth a spark of fire, as he
spoke of the Interior. They could not understand
enthusiasm in any pure undertaking, and they
laughed one day in May ’29 at Government
House—Sturt had been back from his first journey
ten days or so, —when Sheriff Macquoid remarked
jestingly “Well, well, we’ll have to build you a
statue now, Sturt, I s’pose?”
“Its sufficient for me to know,” said the
Lxplorer, “that I led where others must follow.
I hey can t forget me, if they would.” Dreamer
and seer as he was, he could not foresee that the
Australian would, however, act on Macquoid’s
idea. lor we have erected the noblest memorial
to him, have we not? What sublimer tribute
could a great spirit crave than to be left entombed
neath a mountain of Neglect by an age in
which every charlatan can have his effigy? But
we are rambling—and Forger Pounce is waiting
to be introduced to the Captain.
* # *
( “H’m, fine morning, your Hon—, I mean
Captain.” The Captain was roused from his
reverie, but not sufficiently to notice Mr. Pounce’s
slip of the tongue. It was a slip for so venerable
a man to address an official as “Your Honor,”
for it at once betokened the ex-convict. To
transports and expirees of the lower order—to the
latter because they could not readily shake off
the subservience of the former stage of their
colonial career- it was second nature to so salute
one of the Great Powers.
“ Beg pardon, sir. Did not catch your words,”
A FELLOW FEELING.
(Overheard in the Zoological Gardens.)
ELEPHANT: “Well, old ship-of-the desert, how does your back feel after the ’olidays?”
responded Sturt, always as courteous to free men
or transports as to private soldiers or officialdom.
Of course, Sturt knew instinctively that Pounce
was an Emancipist. Had he been free on landing
he must have met him before.
“ I was only taking: the liberty of wishing you a
good morning, Captain.”
“Oh, good morning,”
“Sad sight, sir !” and Pounce waved his hand
feelingly towards the iron-gang.
“Some great ruffians, there, sir!” he went on
as the Captain remained silent. “ Ah, sir, how
awful are the consequences of crime !”
“ Very !” said the Captain, drily. “ But were
you wishing to speak to me about anything in
particular. You know me, but I .”
“Don’t know me! Of course not, sir. But
then, if I may be permitted to say so, it is a pride
and a pleasure for everyone here to know by sight
at least the heroic ”
“You are not permitted to say anything of the
kind.” And Sturt, swinging his light rattan,
turned his head away. He had taken the measure
of his man.
“ Oh, Ido trust no offence, sir. I—l—ventured
to intrude a remark.”
“ Come, sir, to business ! I suppose you had an
object in addressing me. ’Mancipists don’t
usually speak to Officers of the Garrison without
some motive.”
The genial Pounce was momentarily discon-
certed. What really was the use of his being
honest and apeing virtuous demeanour and so
forth, if an official eye could in a moment see
through his mask and penetrate to the expiree t
“It is true, sir, lam a ’Mancipist!” he at last
found strength to say, “ but my little difficulty
with the law, Captain Sturt, was, believe me,
more—more to my credit than to my shame.”
“I cannot understand what your concern is
with me, therefore I am not interested in your
conflict with the law.” The Captain was here, of
course, strictly official, but none was readier than
he to avert from some poor transport the conse-
quences of a hasty and erroneous conviction, once
he was convinced injustice had been done.
“I crave your pardon, sir—l should, p’r’aps
have disclosed my business. Of course, I have
business, sir ; otherwise I would not have dared to
trespass upon your notice. I humbly desire to
offer my services to your Honor ” —he might as
well out with the title now—“ as a transcriber for
the press of the account of your valuable ”
“No compliments ! ”
“Your book, then, sir. It is known all over
Sydney that your journals are soon to be pub-
lished.”
“ Well, they will, and Ido wish assistance. But
what are your qualifications ! Are you a reporter ?
Are you Watts or O’Shaughnessy ? ”
“Neither, sir. Pm” He stopped, to
fumble for his card-case.
“ Can you write legibly, condense, punctuate,
and so on ? ”
“Most competently, Captain Sturt, I assure
you. And permit me to hand your Honor my
card.”
With a graceful bow and dignified flourish of
his white hand, he withdrew from his case and
placed in Captain Sturt’s extended fingers, this :
Timothy Pounce,
Writer and Forger,
Macquarie-street.
Documents of all kinds
forged and uttered. Pardons
a speciality.
The Captain took it, glanced at it, then at
Pounce, and with a harsher stridence in his tones
and a reddening flush over the bronze his cheeks
had brought back from the sun-dried Unsettled
Districts, demanded:
“ Pounce ? You are Pounce, are you ? ”
“Y—es, sir!” Pounce was startled. The
Captain’s polite aspect had changed ominously,
that was obvious. But what had he said or done
that could have associated his name in Sturt’s
mind with something discreditable. He could not
on the instant recall any act of his —at least, any
that had received publicity —which should have
occasioned the alteration in the Captain’s judg-
ment of him.
“ Pounce, the forger ? ”
In holy horror at the imputation, Mr. Pounce
lifted his hands ! How could his Honor think
anything of the sort ? He was simply a writer —a
mere writing clerk, open to do all things honor-
able and artistic that were possible to the pen. It
was true “his little difficulty at home” had been
termed forgery, but he was merely the victim of
misplaced confidence in a friend who had, by way
of illustrating to an admiring circle his, Pounce’s,
artistic penmanship, induced him to write two or
three well-known names on a piece of blank paper.
How could he know that his base friend would
turn the paper into an order for transferring bank-
stock? Mr. Pounce grew pathetically eloquent
as, in flowing periods he told to the listening
officer, the story of the cruel way in which Fate
had treated him, and how nobly he had striven
to retrieve the position which the Law and not
his own misdoing had wrested from him.
“No, sir !” he cried with a thrilling quiver in
his voice, and with an admirably-successful effort
at squeezing two tear-drops from behind the white
handkerchief which he held to his face. “No,
sir ! lam no forger! I revere the laws of my
country too much to become one, to say nothing
of the penalty attached to so heinous a crime !
Call me a ’Mancipist if you like, Captain Sturt—
I can’t help being that, but don’t, your Honor,
pray—pray—term—me a forger.”
Hamilton Hume used to declare Sturt had no
sense of humour. That might be so; but certainly,
as he patiently listened to the plausible old sinner,
he relaxed the stiff lines of his face somewhat.
As well he might! He had never before seen
Pounce, but the artistic excellence of that brilliant
“penman’’was well known to him “by repute,”
and he was consequently in a position to gauge at
its true worth Mr. Pounce’s touching speech in
defence.
* * *
“ You’re not a forger, then, Mr. Pounce?”
“No, sir—indeed—no—sir.”
“But I have had it from your own hand that
you are!”
Pounce was dumbfounded. He had been, he
was convinced, exceedingly cautious in all his
correspondence and had never, so far as he could
recollect, written a word that could be construed
into the statement that he was a professional
forger. He had not looked at the card he had
handed it to the Captain, and could not suspect,
of course, that Sturt had gleaned his knowledge
from Copperplate’s humorous little invention.
He was forced to reply, however, when “True
Charlie ” —Sturt was so known among the gar-
rison and the transports—repeated the assertion.
“I have had it from your own hand, sir, I say,
How dare you contradict me !”
“ May I—l beg, sir—l would humbly submit,
Captain, you must —be mistaken. With
deference, sir, I submit so, sir.” Pounce got
bolder as he found words, although a sinking of
the heart warned him that Sturt, the model of
veracity in a lying society, would not speak so
emphatically without having full reason for his
accusations,
“ Mistaken ! No, sir !” Sturt grew sterner in
voice and feature as he retorted. “I am never
mistaken in dealing with men of your stamp.
I’ve been a friend to the ’Mancipists when they
obeyed the law on release, but when they know-
ingly set the law at defiance, and flaunt their
defiance in the face of the authorities, then my
duty bids me mete out to them punishment.” As
he ended, he drew from the breast-pocket of his
undress jumper a letter-case in which he placed
Pounce’s card, and, from his vest—a whistle. The
hero sank into the police-constable. He blew the
whistle.
Ho not discredit Sturt that he did this duty.
lor that, the System was responsible—the System
made men like him and Franklin, and
Mitchell under-gaolers. They were penal officers
first ; explorers, heroes, thereafter.
* * *
At the sound of the whistle there came
running up a couple of soldiers and a Civil side
overseer. Poor Pounce, on the first impulse
turned to fly. Something, he knew not what, had
brought the fair-seeming edifice of reputation
which he had striven to erect, suddenly to the
ground, and he felt for a second that the game
was up. But second, and cooler thoughts came
to him, whicli bade him see the trouble through.
As, indeed, he could scarcely help doing, inas-
much as :
“ Stop, sir, or I bid them fire !” fell from Cap-
tain Sturt’s lips ; and the “ readying” of the fire-
locks rang a dull second to the officer’s sharp
treble. So Mr. Pounce, writer and forger,
stopped—stopped and smiled as though, after all,
it was only his little joke the whole affair.
The smile vanished quickly, though, as Captain
Sturt ordered the Overseer to arrest Pounce, to
search him at once, and to take him and the
effects found upon his person to the Barracks.
“I will lay the charge there, Overseer. Will it
weaken the gang-guard to send a man with him
and yourself. ”
The man capped and responded : “ I’m ’fraid it
'viH. We’ve a desperate lot there, sir, and are
short-handed as ’tis. It would be bad ’miff for
me to go, sir, let alone a Private. To weaken us,
Cap’n, is just to ax ’em to bolt.”
The most repulsive task which ever fell within
the bounds of Sturt’s duty was never evaded by
him. So
“ Search the prisoner, give me his papers, and
I will take him myself.”
Pounce felt he needed all the moral support
which he could derive from his personal dignity
and his unsullied conscience during the trying
moments that followed. For the Overseer’s
hands, felicitously expert at such work, and
accustomed to find half-figs of tobacco and iron-
filing tools in the most unexpected places, ran up
his person and ran down his person, into his
pockets, into his hat-lining, into his very boots.
With this result
A large sheet of parchment.
A small sheet of stiff paper.
A pocket-book.
Half-a-dozen letters, some in his own hand-
writing, apparently about to be despatched, and
others addressed to himself, very evidently from
clients.
A watch and bunch of seals, a tooth-pick—very
natty in his personal habits was Pounce — and a
card-case —which carried on its outside
Wimotf[y jounce,
and inside a number of visiting-cards. That
unfortunate card-case !
The documents and effects were, with a salute,
passed over by the Overseer to Captain Hturt, who
took them, and pointing with his cane to Hyde
Park Barracks, simply said “ March ! ”
And Mr. Pounce, calling once more to his aid
the Spirit of Integrity, moved three paces ahead
of the Captain in the indicated direction.
And Captain Sturt, the owner of a deathless
name, followed his prisoner !
To such base uses, in the accursed days of Old
Sydney, and Old Hobart, and Old Moreton Bay,
did the System, invented by the Devil in his
mundane residence at No. 14 Downing-street, *
put English gentlemen in whose breasts beat the
hearts of Drake and Raleigh. The soul that
burst the heat-forged bonds which held the Un-
known Australia from the touch of human
interests, and that brother-soul which shattered
the frozen fetters of the Arctic, were, in the wiles
of the Devil, allotted by his creatures, the Men of
the System, the task of controlling convicts.
Sturt and Franklin loathed their task, but per-
formed it. Nothing of its shamefulness attaches
to them. They sought to dignify their work, and
if they failed, the fault lay with the work that
could not be dignified. But what of their Masters,
the Eminent British Statesmen ? The future
will make answer in voiceless scorn. The hand
can frame an expression so infinite in its contempt
that no syllabled utterance can vie with its de-
basing significance. The New Australia will
address that gesture to the memory of the Men of
the System.
* * *
We shall not trouble you, dear readers,
with the complete accounts of the subsequent pro-
ceedings, legal and otherwise. Important though
they were to Friend Timothy, they might not, in
the expansiveness of their detail, interest you.
We shall content ourselves, and we trust you, by
a very brief presentation of the trial of the issues
in Rex v. Pounce, the same being— (l) Larceny of
one sheet of parchment of the value of five shil-.
lings currency; (2) feloniously receiving the before-
mentioned sheet of parchment, supposed to be
stolen; and (3) the forgery to the twice before-
mentioned sheet of parchment of the signature of
his Excellency the Governor, Ralph Darling, and
* In convict days, the official residence of the Secretary
of State for the Colonies. It was in a room in this house
that Wellington and Nelson met for the first, if not the
only, time in their lives.

Now, a blank form of the kind was presumably
never to be found outside of a certain locker in
the room of the Clerk of the Council, except when
in course of preparation for the Governor’s signa-
ture. There was a rigorous regulation which was
supposed to prevent access by convicts or ’Manci-
pists to the place of keeping of departmental
“ blanks,” and coupling the presumption and the
ivgula'.ion, with the fact that the blank pardon
was on Pounce’s person, the jury of officers had
not the least difficulty in finding that Mr. Pounce
was guilty of receiving a stolen sheet. Eor which
offence he was then and the.re awarded seven
years’ transportation He should have got four-
teen, but Judge Dowling probably let him off the
second seven years because he was to bo hanged
on the final accusation. A most tender-hearted
and considerate judge was that dear, old gentle-
man.
Upon God and his country arraigning him upon
the third charge the accused pleaded “Not guilty !”
in the touchingest of tones. That Providence and
his fellow-men could so misjudge him as to sup-
pose him capable of so terrible a crime cut him to
the heart! That was the sentiment made audible
in his plaintive reply to the Clerk of Arraign’s
interrogation. Ah! Fate is often cruel to the
best of men !
During that part of the Crown’s case which
was made up of Secretary Macleay’s evidence
that he had never signed the pardon, and that he
was authorised by His Excellency to state that he
had not done so, the prisoner was gallant and
composed. But his admirable demeanour was
suddenly broken up by a word from Captain
Sturt, who submitted himself to becross examined
Even the best of men make mistakes.
“May I ask, Captain Sturt,” respectfully ques-
tioned the accused, “what you meant by saying on
the morning of my arrest that I had communicated
to you with my own hand that I was a forger ?”
Now, Sturt, in his testimony-in-ehief, had made
no allusion to the visiting-card. It may have
been from forgetfulness, it may have been from
kindness, but so it was. He might, if he had
so chosen, have abstained, through pity, from
telling the whole truth, because, you must know,
he was not placed on his oath. A garrison officer
seldom was placed on his oath in those days in giving
witness in the institutions which were ludicrously
termed the Courts of Justice. Which was an
arrangement very convenient to the officers gener-
ally; to the defendant, or accused, rarely.
The Captain, in answer, took from his envelope-
case the card—
Timothy Pounce,
Writer and Forger,
Macquarie-street,
Documents of all kinds
forged and uttered. Pardons
a speciality.
and said ; “The prisoner, your Honor, introduced
himself to me by this card which I submit for
your Honor’s inspection.”
A moment of curious silence in the court, while
the card was handed up. Pounce forgot his
posturing, and wondered what the devil was
coming next. He had thought of—and provided
for—most possibilities, but this out-ran his judg-
ment.
His Honor looked at the card, looked at Pounce,
took a half-turn in his chair, to let the light fall-
upon the bit of pasteboard, and read out with a
labored distinctness : “ Timothy Pounce, Writer
and Forger. Pardons a speciality .” Then he drew
a deep breath and said, “ Well! ” Nearly every-
body in Court said “Well!” too, sotto-voce. Only
Timothy thought it was 111 Very! He was
staggered.
“ I know —nothing—of that card ! ” he managed
to say at last.
“The witness has declared it ! ” pronounced the
Judge. “Do you challenge Captain Sturt’s evi-
deuce ? 55
Of course he didn’t. What prisoner would dare
to impugn the veracity of an officer of the garrison,
especially “ True Charlie’s ? ”
“ May I suggest, your Honor, that the prisoner
should be permitted to see the card ? ” said Sturt.
The card was handed down, and to Pounce. He
took it eagerly—those in court held in their
breath, anxious not to lose a jot of the words
with which the old rascal would explain away the
damning inscription—and then, with a wild im-
petuosity of utterance, he shouted to where
Copper-Plate Josephs, present as an “ expert wit-
ness,” was sitting :
“ You devil, this is some of your work !”
* * *
“It was very indecorous, that remark,
said Judere Dowling, and “particularly in
decorous,” he further called it, when Copper-Plate
Josephs was put upon the stand and swore he had
no knowledge of the card.
“Do you mean to say, witness,” cross-examined
Pounce, “ that you did not engrave that card ?” i
“Ido. But supposing I did, you must have

given me the order. I couldn’t engrave cards for
your use without your knowledge, could I ? ”
The court laughed at the poser. On the restora-
tion of order :
“Did you not, when I left you my card-case,
engrave this card just to do me harm and put it
with my own ones ! ”
“No !” swore Witness—and Perjurer—Josephs.
“Again, no!”
The Attorney-General, prosecuting for the
Crown, here rose. “I submit, y’r Honor, the
pris’ner should be allowed to insult the
witness in this way. The witness, lam glad to
be informed by the police, is earning his living
honestly. And, further, y’r Honor, I can pro-
duce evidence, if y’r Honor thinks desirable, to
prove, first, that pris’ner at the bar carries on an
illicit trade in forged pardons, tickets and passes,
and, second, that a number of cards, precisely
similar to this, were found on his person in the
card-case to which he refers. It is quite obvious,
y’r Honor, that he has used these cards to prose-
cute his illegal and immoral business, and that
the presenting of his card to Captain Sturt was the
careless act of a criminal made reckless and over-
confident by long immunity.” .
Of course, after this pronouncement, it was only
a matter of minutes for Prisoner Timothy Pounce
to be sentenced to death. Which was done in a
very affecting fashion, indeed, his Honor dropping
at least three tears. He never gave more than
three to a man; a woman got five ; he was especi-
ally affable to the sex.was Puisne Judge Dowling.
# * #
The trial was on a Tuesday ; in the estab-
lished routine of things, Pounce would visit
George-street on Friday morning alive, and
return dead. That the routine was interrupted
was due to a conversation which took place on
Wednesday between a certain high official and the
Condemned.
“ If you and the other gentlemen of the staff
let me be hanged, sir,” declared Pounce, “ within
an hour of ray execution there will be placed in
Dr. Wardell’s hands a full statement, with
proofs, of all the forgeries of land-deeds, and
contract-forms, and pay-warrants which I have
done for you and others. Moreover, I shall tell
the crowd as much as I can on the gallows.”
“ But—we can’t, Pounce—the Governor’s away
at Windsor, and will not return before Friday
noon.”
“ And what’s my time ? The usual, I suppose,
sir?”
“Yes -Nine !”
Mr. Pounce, no longer blue-coated and wlute-
linened, but in hodden grey—and irons—mused.
“Well, I see how to do it! You oblige me
with a sheet of the Council’s paper, and arrange
to get the document to Sheriff Macquoid, and I’ll
forge my reprieve. Then you arrange for ratifica-
tion when the Governor returns, three hours
later.”
Pounce died in his bed at Richmond,
V.D.L., in 1843.

Smith, the Mormon prophet, that Brigham Young
once had nine children born to him in one week.
It is related of the famous Red Prince
that he used to smoke two cigars at a time in a
speci ally-constructed cigar-holder.

Disraeli in his Wycombe candidatures, though
Wellington and Lyndhurst urged them to give
way.

brother is the Australian shearing-machine in-
ventor.

the train free.

Sir Alfred Stephen and Cardinal Moran
were present in the Ruin Chamber for a consider-
able time during the last sitting of the Conven-
tion, and sat side by side. As usual, Law and
Religion were in at the death of Justice.
An American writer says ; —Stanley’s lec-
ture course is a distinct and decided fizzle. He is
a very uninteresting speaker, and he is completely
overshadowed by the brash behaviour of his
mother-in-law, who declares that “ Henry is the
most docile man on earth. ”
London Lord Mayor Savory’s plagiarised
sermon was not stolen by Savory himself, but by
the man whom he employed to write the sermon
for him (though Savory published the thing as his
own). The world is ruled by shams. Behind
all to-day is the skeleton in the cupboard, the
ghost.
The “Professor Parker” now exhibiting
his swordmanship in N.S.W. must not be con-
founded with the “ Professor Parker” (husband of
Rose Beaumont, the singer) who appeared in Aus-
tralia years ago. This latter Parker, it is interest-
ing to know, was the original of Sergeant George
in “Bleak House.” He was killed while riding
in the Gippsland Ranges.
It is said that the denouement of Rudyard
Kipling’s latest story, “ The Light that Failed” —
the blindness falling upon Hick Heldar—was
based on Kipling’s own experience. A Bulletin
man has made a calculation that if Kipling had
been paid for the reproductions of his stories in
the larcenous Australian press—and our con-
tributor sees only a moiety of that influential
section of the newspaper world —Kipling would
have been the richer by over £5OO. As to which,
more anon.
Migraine.
Nervous neadaches and neuralgia are the first,
surest, and most reliable symptoms of approach-
ing decline of physical health. They are distinctly
nerve pains, an infallible telegraph message prog-
nosticative of approaching physical weakness.
They are intimations that the strength is being
impaired. Over-work of any description must
result in enervation and enfeeblement of the
corporeal structure. This wear and tear (termed
by scientists destructive metabolism) may be ,
brought about by worry, excessive mental or
physical labour, by continual attention to business,
by bad food, bad air or any acute sickness, the
duties of maternity, the strain of motherhood,
dissipation, and many other causes. The proper
remedy is Clements Tonic. This must and will
cure the worst and most severe cases. This is a
positive fact; it has done it, and will do it again
always. We are thus emphatic because we know
we are talking facts, which the evidence of dis-
interested persons proves. Mr. Henry A. Crane,
a gentleman well-known all over the colony,
writes ; Dear sir, —Tor the last seven years I
have been a victim to the most excruciating agony
from neuralgia, and also suffered from extreme
nervousness. I tried hundreds of so-called
specifics, but without avail ; and, being a com-
mercial traveller, am particularly liable to colds
from exposure to draughts, &c. ; a slight one even,
always fled to my facial nerves, and caused
neuralgic pains, preventing sleep and causing
excessive sympathetic headache. I saw Clements
Tonic advertised, procured a bottle, and in two
days tlie pain was completely cured, a consum-
mation I had never expected and am deeply
thankful for same. This was four months ago,
and from then till now I have never had the
slightest twinge of the excruciating torture I used
to have. You may make whatever use of this
you like. W. H. East, Esq,, Stanthorpe, Dulwich
Hill, N.S.W., suffered a martyrdom of headaches,
due to disordered liver and indigestion, frequently
having to leave his work in the middle of the day.
He took a course of Clements Tonic, and writes ;
Stanthorpe, Dulwich-street, Petersham. —Dear
si r> —]?or years I have been a great sufferer from
headache, which has at times so prostrated me
that I have been totally unable to attend to my
work. I have consulted the most skilled phy-
sicians in Sydney, and spent considerable money
in fees and medicine. I, however, obtained only
temporary relief, as they treated the effect, and
not the cause. I was recommended Clements
Tonic, and determined to test its efficacy, and I
am very grateful for the good results which have
followed its use. I took five bottles, and am
wonderfully improved, and have had no return of
those excruciating headaches since taking that
valuable remedy. Results like this must encourage
your laudable efforts to alleviate the sufferings of
your fellow men ; and you have my sanction to
publish this. Sydney Cunningham, Esq., 70,
Watkin-street, Newtown (N.S.W.), writes as fol-
lows -.—Dear Sir,—Having been a long sufferer
from neuralgia, which weakened me considerably,
and using various remedies without benefit, I was
induced to give Clements Tonic a trial, and am
pleased to inform you that the result exceeded my
utmost expectations, and after taking five large
bottles I feel better than ever I did in my life
before. My wife and other members of our
family have used it with equal success, and I can
strongly recommend it to others suffering in alike
manner, and offer you this unsolicited testimonial
to use as you think fit.” Reader, can any other
medicine show such a record of cures as Clements
Tonic? We think not.

The progress made by the Mutual Life
Insurance Company of New York during the year
1890 is fully exhibited in the figures which appear
in the business columns of this week’s issue.
During the year the Company issued 49,188 new
policies for insurances amounting to £33,538,740
19s. lid. and it is stated that this amount is the
largest volume of new business transacted in any
one year since the organization of the Company
in 1843. The policies in force at the close of the
year numbered 206,055 covering insurances amount-
ing to £132,963,930. The payments to policy-
holders for the year amounted to £3,536,683 6s.
lid. The assets of the Company, all invested in
undoubted securities, are quoted at the prodigious
sum of £30,657,283 11s. Bd. After providing full
and stringent reserves for obligations of every
kind, the Company reports a surplus of over two
millions of pounds sterling. These figures should
prove comforting to the Australian policy-holders
with “ the largest and wealthiest insurance
organization in the world.” The principal Aus-
tralian Agency of the Company is at 131 Pitt-
street, Sydney, and is conducted by Major Z. C.
Rennie as General Manager.

Society
The affable millionaire, ex-Mayor Burde-
kin, of Sydney—he resigned in order that he might
travel—has depaited for London amid such pomp
and splendour as on occasions of the kind always
surround gilded public men who are not absolute
curmudgeons. The Burdekin is understood to be on
the track of a baronetcy or earldom or marqui-ate,
or some such little trifle. Not long ago Governor
Carington stepped into a city bank, apparently
on business bent, and was followed by Burdekin,
upon whose arrival much hat-lifting and pump-
handling took place. The pair conversed for a
few minutes, and in the course of the confab
Burdekin, with “Excuse me, your Lordship,” set
Baron Charlie’s beautiful blue tie straight, Lady
Carington apparently not having run her pretty
eyes over it that morning. It strikes us
that a millionaire who is on tie-straightening terms
with a Baron, who in his turn is on t’e-straighten-
ing terms with the Heir Apparent, should have
a very excellent chance of “ getting something ”
from the Colonial Office.
# * *
By the way, we hope the ex-Mayor will
remember that it is not considered the cheese in
London West End circles to wear a big cigar in
your jaw if you are driving with a lady. We
mention this to prevent any such awful occurrence
as the public fumigation of Victoria Regina et
Imporatr x with Partaga-smoke.
* * *
Alderman W. p. Manning has unani-
mously been elected Mayor of Sydney. Mr.
Manning is a professional financier in a very large
way and is the Australian representative of some
of the wealthiest of the British investors. As he
bears the reputation of being both straight
and inqu sitive, it is likely that the finances
of the corporation will be put on a thorough
business footing during his term of office. In
public Mr. Manning always eats his peas with a
fork, but it is not known whether his influence
will prevent apple-tait being eaten at the Town
Hall with soup-spoons.
# « #
It happened quite recently. Governor Jer-
sey, with “ Joe ” Carruthers, and another N.S.W.
Minister of the Crown, found themselves together
at an institution where there wai a visitors’ book
to be signed and comment to be made. His Ex.
led off, and having affixed his signature, putin three
lines of favourable comment opposite thereto.
Carruthers was the next to sign, and he, of course,
put his name two lines below “ Jersey’s,” in order
to bring it clear of the overhanging comment.
That was as it should b° ; but then the third man
came along to sign, and he was mentally disturbed
at seeing Carruthers’name next to the Governor’s.
He thought that » his place, seeing that he had
been shadowing H.E. all day, so he acted ac-
cordingly. He ignored the fact that the “turn-
over” of Jersey’s comment required, in due form,
two blank lines to be left below his name, and
straightway splodged, right under Jersey’s and
before Carruthers’, his own immortal monniker.
Name ? Quite unnecessary.
# *
The conduct of the undergraduate yahoos,
or “Liver Push,” of Sydney University at
Saturday’s Commemoration outvied the most
doughty deeds of the Woolloomooloo or Shepherd’s
Bush brigade. These gowned and privileged
hoodlums showed the utmost discourtesy to every-
one present, insulted and silenced the octogenarian
Chancellor and addressed Lady Jersey in a style
which will probably induce her and most other
ladies to stay away from Commemoration next
year. As the University authorities appear in-
capable of leading out these larrikins to gaol by
the ear, the State should step in with a few
muscular policemen as the alternative of with-
drawing all subsidies from an institution which,
instead of being a seat of culture, appears to be a
seed-plot of privileged blackguardism and a train-
ing school for the N.S.W. Parliament. The antics
of the Sydney undergraduates are of the most
hackneyed and undiverting type and are merely
misc-nhle exaggerations of the unmannerly pranks
of he r English prototypes. Up to date, no
Syc ne/University undergraduate has ever said
an original or witty thing at Commemoration,
except once ; on the occasion when a man and a
girl went up together and solemnly stood before
the Chancellor to take the same degree and some-
one said with a loud voice “ I will! ”

Mr. R. B. Pell, of Broken Hill, N.S.W.,
has just been gazetted town and district coroner.
“ R. B. Pell” is advertised as referee at Felix
Tanner’s 40 days’ fast; “R. B. Pell” was also
stake-holder and referee at the Burge and
M‘Carthy slugging match, when the men fought
19 or 20 rounds, and “at the finish Burge got a
belt under the jaw that sent him to sleep for three
minutes,” There are evidently two people at
Broken Hill named R. B. Pell —or else the old
original R.B.P. went to the prize-fight in his
coronial capacity.
* # *
London Truth has informed its readers
that there is every prospect of Broken Hill Consols
touching ten guineas (as though they were gold
watches or oil-paintings) on the London market.
From which we assume that somebody has already
bought a lot at fifty “ bob.”
* * *
Hawk states that Governor Hopetoun
will shortly abdicate in favour of Governor
Onslow, he (Hopetoun) having been a failure. It
is Mr. De Moleyns, Governor Hopetoun’s brother-
in-law, who has been the failure, and if you don’t
believe it just enquire at Toorak.
* * #
Hopetoun is a veritable artesian well of
wisdom, and a perennial flow of sparkling ideas.
The other day he found himself called upon to
offer advice concerning agriculture, and virgin
soil, and other matters of that description. Now,
the larger the subject the better for Hopetoun—
give him plenty of scope and his lordship becomes
immense. With knitted brows and an eye peer-
ing into the distant future, he said, calmly, deli-
berately, and with the air of a thinker who has
just struck it rich —“Every rood of land in Vic-
toria must be made the most of.” A gasp of
astonishment, followed by a loud peal of applause,
proclaimed that another of Hopetoun’s proverbs
had sunk deep into the hearts of the people.
* * #
James Johnston, who, while in a state of
mental depression, consequent on the discovery of
a big forgery he had committed, killed his wife
and children at Ballarat, and then almost fatally
cut his throat, has been convicted and sentenced
to death. He was carried into court on a stretcher.
It is pretty certain that he will not be executed —
although one can never reckon what the Executive
—generally wooden-headed in such eases —may do.
The question of Johnston’s mental responsibility

ANXIOUS INFANT: “And where is
the seat of Government likely to
be, Old Man ? ”
PARKES : “Wherever Hi sit, my little
dear, and don't you forget it! ”
at the time is,-in the opinion of sensible people,
completely settled by the fact that he admittedly
did his very level best to kill himself. If Johnston
had killed himself without killing his family, the
coroner’s jury would, of course, have found a
verdict of suicide while temporarily insane; and
surely it is no proof of a man’s sanity that he did
kill his wife and children. If the law hangs
Johnston, Johnston will die for the logical pur-
pose of encouraging homicidal cranks who have
committed murder, to be sure and slit their own
weasands in the right place.

* * #
American girls are alleged to be finding
fault with women doctors for their want of delicacy.
The nicest he-leeches always used to drop their
voices and look dreamy when certain mysterious
matters came under discussion, whereas the prac-
tical doctoresses talk of Nature to one of their
own sex as though it weren’t in the least bit
indecent. Briefly, the transatlantic nymph has
no longer any excuse for giggling during medical
treatment, nor is there any joy in sharing strange
secrets with a practitioner who can’t make her
feel so dreadfully awkward, my dear, every time
she meets her in society. Therefore, something
seems wanting in the life of a high-toned Yankee
maiden, and now that she can’t have her pulse
felt by a manly, lingering paw, she has loft off
experiencing a strange sinking sensation so often
as she used to in the old, happy days.—
Lay-’em-out Price and his melodramatic
Mounted Rifles are sure to be a success at the
military tournament in London. The cockneys
will treat them as a new Australian edition of
Buffalo Bill and the cowboys.
* # #
One incident of Sir George Grey’s visit to
Sydney has not yet been fully recorded. It was
the funniest thing seen in public for a good while
and occurred at the meeting between Sir John
Robertson and Grey at the historically enthusiastic
reception given to the latter by the Maorilanders
in Sydney Town Hall. Neither of the two
Knights knew the identity of the other, but old
Jack’s flowing locks and the cheers of the crowd
indicated that he was a popular old fossil of some
kind, and when he went to sit down next to Sir
George the latter put out his hand as if to wel-
come him. But Sir Jack hadn’t been introduced,
you know, and being perhaps in one of his
tantrums quietly turned away—didn’t like liber-
ties. Then people laughed, and he had another
look at Grey, and they both looked at one another.
In a few moments they began to whisper, and
finally (after having sat together for about five
minutes) they started to shake hands vigorously—
which dumb show set the house in a roar. From
that out Sir Jack was the most hearty in his
applause of his brother veteran. In fact, so
boisterous did he become that at the close c f the
proceedings he started to rush everybody forward
to present them to the great pro-consul, and
amongst others took the hands of Mrs. Seymour
George (Sir George Grey’s own niece) and her
daughter to lead them up to him ! At this the
Maorilanders simply screamed.
* # #
During his final speech in Convention, Sir
George Grey made a trifling slip by referring to
Attorney-General Clark as the representative of
“VanDieman’s Land.” There was a guffaw at
this from the assembled Convention’sts, the
cachinnators overlooking the circumstance that
for thirty years of strenuous official life the
veteran had been in the habit of accurately writ-
ing and uttering the ancient designation of the
island colony. Their breach of good manners
provokes the retort that Grey was the one speaker
who never tripped into a barbarism of speech.
Even the rhetorical Deakin, who stood next to
the MaoriJander in point of precision of phrase,
fell more than once into painful solecisms. As for
Parkes, his speech on the motion to adopt the
Constitution Bill was perhaps the most aifch-less
and aitch-ful that he has ever delivered. Which
is saying a good deal.
* *
Petherick has sent us a copy of “A
Colonial Reformer,” Rolf Boldrewood’s latest-
published story. At least that is what the re-
viewers call it. We recognise in Mr. Ernest
Neuchamp, the hero of the book, however, a very
old friend indeed. We wish Rolf, in addition to
being so exact in his Australian colouring, were
not so dreadfully anachronistic. His books pro-
fess to be pictures of Australian life, but strict
attention to the mode of the period is
the essence of faithful portraiture, and in the
whole series of his works, chatty, vigorous
Boldrewood too often errs in making an episode
or a character move in a sphere chronologically
impossible.

A newly-married woman, the bride of a
fortnight, named Priscilla Petrich, committed
suicide in Sydney a few days ago, because, poof
soul, she couldn’t cook. If all other wives simi-
larly situated were to follow her sad example
there would be a terrible number of gaps in tins
year’s census-papers. The danger is not imminent,
however.
® # #
The Women’s Literary Society of New
South Wales has made its second public appear-
ance, as host at an invitation conversazione. If
literature can be encouraged among women by the
formation of a society it is high time that some
such movement were pushed forward in Sydney.
Women who can write are generally glad enough
to write for money, and yet, curiously enough,
The Bulletin does not remember having pub-
lished six purely literary original contributions
from N.S.W. ladies in over ten years. Nor does
it recollect having seen in any of its N.S.W. con-
temporaries any work from local female hands
the privilege of publishing which it envied.
Leaving out of the question members of the fair
sex who are paid salaries for more or less newsy
and compilatory work, and who may or may not
be capable of doing anything better, we know of
but one N.S.W. lady whose copy commands
money wherever offered in Australia, and that
lady is an Englishwoman who writes equally
well in both French and English, prose and
verse, and who has been in Australia for
about five years. On the other hand Queensland
has at least two or three excellent women
writers ; Victoria also ; likewise South Australia,
Tasmania and Maoriland.

n Bouverie-street, Mary Ellen King came in and knocked

Never again shall we write the phrase :
“He was no one’s enemy but his own.” In future
we say this : “He neglected life’s solids for its
fluids.”
* # «.
Her Gracious’s ironclad Penguin has been
a profitable speculation for the Australian police-
man. At every port which that vessel has visited
every man bolted who could possibly get away,
and for each deserter there was offered the cus-
tomary reward of £3 —which is increased to £6 if
the missing individual doesn’t turn up within the
first day. When the absentee is recovered he is
locked up for a few weeks or months in a cell, and
his pay is stopped, and in addition he is debited
with the reward paid for his recovery, so that ho
is generally in debt to Victoria by the Grace of
God, &c., and that sovereign lady has a good deal
of trouble and difficulty in collecting the bill.
The band will now strike up “ Rule Britannia.”
THOSE DELICATE GIRLS.
The delicate girl has been heard to declare,
With a shake of her scandalised tresses,
That when she is ailing she cannot abear
The treatment of rude doctoresses ;
Oh, whether she’s plump, or as lean as a goat
That staryes in the time of a famine —
Whate’er her complaint, she’ll religiously vote
For a man to come in and “examine.”
A creature in skirts (very likely in specs.),
May be of diseases a curer,
But maidens find folks of the opposite sex
More innocent-minded and purer ;
There’s something so nice in the gentleman’s
touch
For a girl in a weakly condition,
Whereas she is never made happy, not much,
By the squeeze of a lady-physician.
Her friend, Doctor Bill, was unmarried and
young,
And smirked as he offered his greeting ;
She’d sigh when he gazed at the tip of her tongue,
Or timed how her bosom was beating ;
And when dearest mamma had tried to explain
How long daughter Lil had been sick, he’d
Turn round very slowly and eye her again
In a way scientific and wicked.
But Mary M.D. calls a shovel a spade,
And isn’t the least sympathetic,
She knows, right away, what is wrong with the
maid,
And orders a pill, or emetic;
She don’t want her patient, in scanty attire,
To show her those natural beauties
The doctor was always so apt to admire
Before he completed his duties.
The delicate girl means to go upon strike
For Bill, as of old. to attend her.
The kind of advice that such sufferers like
Comes best from the masculine gender.
Yet ice, who are up to the tricks of mankind—
We husbands and fathers, or couriers—
Think Mary M.D. was by Nature designed
To doctor all Mother Eve’s daughters.
E. F.
THE LOVE THAT LASTS.
I do not love a maiden fair;
Her tresses shame the carrots,
Her lips are not a happy pair,
Her nose is like a parrot’s;
She has not got the witching eye
That people write a verse on—
But yet she is, -which none deny,
A capital young person.
In spite of all her “ blooming cheek ”
Her face is rather pasty,
Although her wit is slow and weak,
Her temper’s strong and hasty ;
They say her form is long and lank :
I’ve heard her called a “rafter ”
Her nice, round figure at the hank
Is what I’m looking after.

* * *
In the smart Australian novel, “ The
Bride from the Bush,” which the Cornhill in
London and several weeklies in what Mr.
Hornung was accustomed to call “the Aus-
twalian Kawlinies ’’—published as “ a work by a
new author ’’—the heroine, Gladys Bligh (the
“Bride”) is a caricature of a living person who
sent Mr. Hornung, the author, about his busi-
ness. Hornung was a tutor at Mossgiel Station,
Riverina, for about eleven months, and spent
another three or four months in Sydney and Mel-
bourne, and it must be said that the local colour
which he managed to impart to his book certainly
indicates a keenness of vision and a clearness of
brain seldom found in the imported tutor, don-
cherknow. Hornung, however, errs —“ lies ”is a
nasty word—in calling Gladys a typical Aus-
tralian girl—we should be glad to think he was
equally inaccurate in depicting high-toned English
society. The society in which, according to him,
judges, members of Parliament, and royalty com-
mingle equals the blackguardism of the Rocks and
Little Bourke-streets—with a gloss on.
* # #
When Premier Ballance was lately ban-
queted by his Wanganui constituents, some joker
lent a dress-suit and gave a guinea ticket to a
local sot known to be very rowdy whe.n drunk.
But the boozer, much to the disgust of his patron,
kept reasonably sober all the time and made no
noise. Next day, however, he pawned the dress-
suit for a pound, sent the ticket to the owner, and
went on a howling spree.
# * *
A Sydney lodginghouse-keeper was very
thankful the other day when one of her boarders
—who intended to leave next day—volunteered to
fill in her census-paper. Having obtained par-
ticulars concerning all in the house, he retired to
his room and described his hostess as “55,
divorced, hashmaker, idiotic, native of Kilkenny,
and a fanatical Roman Catholic.” A very swell
“master-hairdresser, who resided in the estab-
lishment, was put down as a “shearer,” and a
Civil Servant, who thinks no small beer of him-
self, was described as “Boots and generally use-
ful.” The fraud was only discovered when the
Census-collector arrived and started to check the
returns.
# * *
A month or two back The Bulletin
wanted to know what the Government were going
to do with the convict who went into Darling-
hurst twenty odd years ago, as a boy under
sentence tor life, now that they know he has in-
herited a large fortune? This prisoner has never
seen the sea, having been born up-country, and
having lived there till his conviction. In all
worldly matters of an honourable kind he must be
a literal innocent. The least the authorities can
do is to take care that when he goes out he does
not exchange his capital for somebody else’s ex-
perience.

* * *
A Melbourne man was brought up the
other day at the Police Court, charged with
travelling without a railway-ticket and using
appalling language to a porter who asked his
address. He acknowledged the ticket, but, with
regard to the swearing, he stated briefly that he
trusted Providence would strike him dead and
blast his remains with lightning if he had sworn
at that crimson porter. So he was acquitted
right there.

The worthy Italians have started a public
subscription to purchase bronze wreaths for two
seamen of H.M.S. Rodney. They are brave
fellows apparently, but if they don’t flinch at the
thought of those wreaths they must be more than
human. Picture Jack ashore, with a yellow-
haired girl on either arm, and the Italian tribute
slipping down over his dexter optic.
# * *
Melbourne Duffy, the Victorian P.M.G.,
has returned from Sydney with a stern resolve to
improve the Melbourne Post-office arrangemen s,
as regards counter business. Hitherto the Mel-
bourne “ registered-letter ” department nymphs
have been studied through a wire network, just as
though they were wild animals, or an aviary or
feathered songsters. For the future they will
beam across the counter in the ordinary way.
Anon, when the building is further enlarged,
Duffy intends to abolish the wooden partitions,
with holes in ’em, that shut off the fair stamp-dis-
nensers from an outside world and make them so
uncommonly suggestive of a peep-show, in due
course their veil of mystery will be torn away by
the official iconoclast, and a fellow who wants to
communicate with Matilda through the post will
no longer feel like a sinner at confessional when
he goes to buy a penny Queen’s-head with gum
behind it.

Charles Shaw, prominent Victorian Free-
mason and erstwhile wholesale jeweller, died last
week. Melbourne business men will remember
him as the hero of a big insolvency case which
threw a brilliant light on the machinery of the
Bank sweating-room.
* # #
From S.M. Herald’s London correspond-
ence :
A good mot is credited to Mr. Haddon Chambers. On
the first night of his new piece, “ The Idler,” he was in-
troduced to the Duchess of Leinster, one of the reigning
beauties of London society, who congratulated him very
warmly on the success of his play, saying that she should
send all her friends to witness it. “In that case, your
grace,” said Mr. Chambers, with a courtly bow, “ the
piece will go on running for ever.”
Nothing succeeds like success, dear friends.
Haddon’s alleged mot (which isn’t original—and
was no doubt communicated to the Herald’s
London correspondent either by the beautiful
Duchess or the little man who made to her what
no little man can possibly make—i.e., a “courtly”
bow) is a poor thing, and we should be .ashamed
to throw it off our chest at the yard-dog, to say
nothing of the Duchess of Leinster. Sheol is full
of men who have tried to advertise themselves on
such ghastly “witticisms.”

Her breath is not exceeding sweet,
Her teeth are black and yellow,
I often think her heavy feet
Were made to kick a fellow ;
Her song is like the nanny-goat’s,
But, put her to the test, O !
And she’ll produce the richest notes
From out her wooden chest , 0 !
A straw for girls with noble necks.
Whose curve our pulses quickens—
One sovereign of the softer sex
Ain’t fifty thousand “thick ’uns.”
Poor envy’s jest but makes me laugh.
Nor in my bosom rankles—
My idol is the golden calf,
And never mind her ankles.
E.F.
THE ROSEHILL SMILE.
In Melbourne’s uppah classes
All the maidens’ eyes are red,
There are tears upon their pillows
As they ruminate in bed,
They 101 l around and languish
In a touching toney style.
And they utter cries of anguish
For his lordship’s toothy smile.
He used to thrill their bosoms
With his simple sandy glance,
He was chiefest of attractions
At the dinner fete or dance;
He always looked so winning
And so destitute of guile
For the sweetest thing in grinning,
Was the toothy Rosehill smile.
At the vice-regaelic function
It was ever to be seen,
So remindful of our childhood
In its innocence serene;
’Twas a feature of the races,
Where we’ll miss it for awhile —
It was just like “making faces ”
Was the toothy Rosehill smile.
At a certain favoured cafe
Where his lordship loved to eat,
His grin was as a sunbeam
Falling brightly on the meat;
When he toyed with his pertaters
In a spirit volatile,
O ! it paralysed the waiters,
Did the toothy Rosehill smile.
Have you ever, gentle reader,
Seen him grace a pnvate-box,
With the angels watching o’er him,
Clad in Worth of Paris frocks?
Or, in circle-seat, delighting
Half-a-dozen nymphs nubile,
Who were femininely fighting
For their darling’s toothy smile?
In Melbourne’s uppah classes
Even wives are looking glum
(As regards that social subject
We had better murmur “ mum
And the poet, too, is weeping
Like a good old crocodile,
For it haunts him when he’s sleeping,
Does the well-remembered smile.
Peculiar Infatuation.
DIFFERENT METHODS OF FOLLOWING THE IN-
JUNCTION “ LOVE ONE ANOTHER.”
Do men ever fall in love with each other?
Women do. Not long ago a young woman in
Jersey was married to a youthful labourer on her
father’s farm. Some time afterwards it was dis-
covered that the husband was a female. The
young wife refused, however, though earnestly en-
treated by her friends, to give up her chosen
consort. The strangest part of the discovery was
the fact that the bride knew her husband was a
woman before she was led to the altar. _
If men do not exhibit this strange infatuation
for one of their own sex, they at least oftentimes
give evidence of the fact that they love one
another. There are many instances on record
where one man has given his life for another.
It is a proud possession—the knowledge that
one has saved a precious human life. Diraboola,
Victoria, is the home of such a happy man. Mr.
y. Hughes, cordial manufacturer, of that town,
August 22, 1890, writes : —“ Four years ago, when
practising for the local sports, I became pros-
trated in health, X was under the impression that
heart disease would cut short my life. My liver
was enlarged, and my doctor ordered me complete
rest from business as well as training. My
kidneys became disordered, and my water was
filled wuth brick-dust and gravel. Finding my
coinplaint, instead of being checked, assumed a
more serious form, I took a friend’s adv’ce, and
started upon a course of Warner’s safe cure.
Almost instantly relief followed, and after the
third or fourth bottle, felt convinced that my
disease, though far advanced, would soon be
cured. The pains in the heart ceased entirely,
the urine became clearer, and the medicine has
wrought an unexpected change in my condition.
I always recommend Warner’s safe cure.”
Friendship expresses itself in very peculiar
ways sometimes ; but the true friend is the friend
in need.
