846 Maoquarie-street North, Sydney, April 15,1891.
My Dear Moorabinda,—
Sydney sassiety - loyal enough to go about shoeless if
vice-royalty only set the example —is now whispering over
its tea-tray that the little Jerseys (who, after all, are “ no
more than schoolgirls, my dear”) are being drafted into
society at a very unconventional period indeed. Races,
banquets, and balls are all adorned by the presence of the
two eldest children, still in short frocks, and there is
likely to be a strike' in various fashionable nurseries
where the budding belles are kept chained up until dear
Gertrude goes off. Whether Lady Jersey turns her hope-
fuls loose in this reckless way because she wishes to give
them a sort of sugar-coated object-lesson on the Horrors
of Bad Breeding, or whether she thinks local matrons
don’t know a debutante when they see her, deponent is
unaware—anyway, there are the young ladies bowing in
their mother's ball-room while society has a vague sense
that its powerful opinion is being flouted. But this isn’t
how' society ought to feel about it by several chalks.
What higher mark of confidence could Lady Jersey pay
the cotton-backed duchesses and gay and festive men-
about-towm who constitute the local Llue-blood than to
let her girls listen to the vapid little cackle of the one and
waltz in the arms of the other?
But while on the subject, I really think that, consider-
ing how badly the Australian girl w'ears, the age
of the average debutante—eighteen or nineteen-
ought to be lessened and Ermyntrude allowed to figure
on ball-programmes at sixteen or seventeen. Even as a
schoolgirl the Australienne has often a weary, dragged
expression, and if you keep her under lock and key when
she is in the morning of her charms (if she has any), you
leave her only one or two years of, conquering kittenish-
ness. For when the rubicon of twenty is passed the
antipodean damsel is a woman, powder, paint, and all.
What do you think of the new society walk ? My
theory is that it is a natural consequence of the New.
market coat and the mannish plain skirt, but, above all,
the fault of that double-breasted jacket. The idea is to
stride as far as you can with each-ahem—ankle, at the
same time slightly raising your shoulders and giving a
bad imitation of the masculine shoulder-swagger to be
observed in all its full beauty in the athletic young man
Your pace, too, must be a cracker. If you want an exact
model of the sideview of the fashionable gait just at
present, take a pair of wide-open scissors and straddle
them violently up and down the table, and then you have
it. Apropos to deportment-is it because a leading
member of the vice-regal staff has a pronounced Grecian
bend that the Sydney dude is circulating round with a
kink in his spine ?
Sydney Ladies’ Sanitary Club met at the Town Hall on
10th inst. with Lady Jersey in th 6 chair, and, of course, a
crowd of members present. It was a lovely sight to see
how, while worshipping at the footstool of Hygeia, they
wore corsets that seemed to be rather worse than a com-
fortable fit. Lady Jersey showed her contempt for the
law of chromatics by appearing in a mauve and violet
frock, blue gloves, and a canary-coloured bonnet, and
opened the meeting in a small, nervous voice, after which
a medico, who seemed to appear under protest, gabbled
through a paper on “Colds,” and then a rush was made
for another room, where the same gentleman held up and
expatiated upon a collection of curry-coloured hygienic
undergear of the most confidential cut, at which your
Sappho blushed up to the roots of her hair and fled pre-
cipitately.
Lady Jersey talks very nicely, but as an orator she’s not
a little tiny patch on Lady Hamilton, Governoress of
Tasmania, who ripples along in a silvery voice without a
gasp or a pause, except at commas and so on. It would
be a pleasure to hear Lady H. giving out her views even
on Imperial Federation or the Moral Aspect of Freetrade.
The second vice-regal hop on 10th inst. was more
successful than the first since fewer guests were bidden)
and the evening was quite fine. The halls of dazzling
light were conventionally decorated by a Sydney florist,
and the electric-light shone brightly everywhere, turning
the pink make-up on many a blushing cheek to a soft
faint mauve. The ball opened with a set of Lancers.
Lord J. danced with Lady Mar .in in soft black silk and
lace, the front cut square and filled in with soft white
fluted mull, diamond ornaments; Lady J., in ciel-blue
armure en traine festooned with flowers which didn’t
exactly match the gown, diamond ornaments and huge
bouquet, graced the dance with Sir George Innes; Lady
Margaret Villiers in pale-grey chiffon had Captain Davis
for a partner ; the other vice-regal daughter in pale fox-
glove pink danced with Mr. Haggard. Mrs. Rupert
Carington, who also took part in the opening dance, wore
a Letty Lind dress of soft grey chiffon over shrimp-pink
silk, diamond ornaments.
“ Why does her husband allow it V” said a woman sitting
next me in a Sydney dress-circle the other evening, look-
ing across at a matron whose decollete was rather
astonishing—“ I thought he was a rabid prude over bare
shoulders.” But to me, the bare-back show of the woman
who is always clothed severely to her ear-lobes when
Augustus is along didn't call for the least surprise.
For once, when I had a tedious wait in a theatre cloak-
room, I gathered how matrons arrange a regular meta-
morphosis in the garments they leave home in. The lady
who introduced me to the trick had a rich, black-beaded
gown, with a deep yoke fore and aft of passementerie, afl
very nice, and according to the lord and master’s ukase,
I have no doubt; but guess my surprise when, in a
twinkle, she unhooked that yoke fore and aft, and tucked
it where the cotton wool usually goes, and stood revealed
in a shrieking see-more jacket! From her fan she took a
cluster of red roses and pinned them in a festive position,
and then, with a final flourish of the powder-pad, she
sailed forth to conquer. What would happen if hubby
chanced to stroll in front on some of these occasions is
quite beyond the conjecture of yours truly.
On the doleful occasion of the popular Mr. Walshe’s
crowded farewell benefit, in Sydney New Masonic Hall,
the trebly-encored beneficiary was miles ahead of his
field as a vocalist. Of the lady soloists, Signora Fabris was
dramatic in “Pace miodio;” Mrs. Vanderveer Greene—the
best-dressed woman on the stage in her tulle and sequins
and saffron roses-contributed two numbers in splendi
voice; Miss Colbourne-Baber sang “Bel Raggio veiy
daintily in Italian style, and Mrs. Riley was best in her
encore “Speed On.” The instrumentalists were Miss
Lottie Hyam and Mr. Alpress (in conjunction) and
Kowalski, none of whom would respond to the double and
treble encores which followed their efforts, though
Kowalski had to bow three times after his “ Maltha
paraphrase. The vanishing tenor goes abroad to put a
final flourish on his top 0.
I want to point my finger at some of the male soloists
at the aforesaid benefit who, in more than one instance,
made their bob and marched off the platform even while
the accompanist was still scrambling through the
harmonious finishing-off. Vocalists should remember
that the man at the piano holds their fate in the hollow
of his hand, as it were, and that a sympathetic accom-
panist deserves a lot of consideration. How different
they- looked from the more gracefully-mannered songsters
who did not take any notice of the applause until the
final chord was struck !
Apropos to farewells, a friend of mine who attended a
Sims Reeves farewell ere she left England twenty years
ago, took a trip ’Ome the other day, and the first concert
she attended was another Sims Reeves farewell! If she
goes back a hundred years hence she may strike against
the same placard—unless the veteran tenor be then con-
sidered rusty enough for an Australian tour.
Rather a fortuitous circumstance in connection with
the late military holocaust at Middle Head was that the
unfortunate Lieutenant Bedford insured his life for a
considerable sum before going into camp. It was all
owing to a cast-iron agent who would not take No. “ I
have no money to buy a policy,” said the doomed man.
“Raise it,” said the agent, and the result was that a
valuable horse was sold and the insurance completed.
The sequel of Widow O’Brien, “ Bridget O’Brien, Esq.,”
at the Garrick, shows the former loveable eccentricity in
several new and raging garments, and a fete-dress of plum
velvet, cream brocade and crystal trimmings, with Paris
bonnet of mauve feathers and gloves en suite. The cos-
tumes are fresh and good and pretty. Grade Whiteford
(by the way, why does she talk like the Olive Berkley
Fauntleroy?) wears a perfect “Fiddle and I” suit —all
brown velvet and frizzed hair—and I saw by the gloating
eyes cf a matron with a small boy brat that he would
hurtle forth to the next juvenile costume ball clothed in
similar robes. There was a good circle the other night,
and a decollete female in a melon-pink empire skirt and
sleeves (she really hadn’t any bodice) whiled away the
fleeting minutes between the “ax” by powdering herself
all over arms, face, and buzzuin with a powder-pad the
size of a boxing-glove.
I have before me at present (though I really do not
deserve it) a strange, awful composition yclept “Under
the Southern Cross,” by “ Vaga.” It is called a serenade,
though the midnight vocalist who would spoil his adored’s
beauty-sleep by singing “ What are the old countries’
fights to you and me ?” would merit all the hull-dogs and
cold douches meted out to him in the comic papers. The
spiric of the thing is Australian-national to the last dot.
Nevertheless, it is “ dedicated to the Countess of Jersey.”
In the “My Jack” revival at Her Majesty’s, Watts
Phillips dresses Dorothy Prescott quietly, and has im.
proved her stage appearance by putting on a little more
colour; and Mrs. Rignold, as Mrs. Prescott, wears a soft
grey and white gown in the last act, which accords well
with her silver hair. A novel feature in the d.c. on
Saturday, was a pretty Japanese lady dressed a VAnglaise,
which means all the colours of the rainbow, and corsets .
but she wore her rounded olive arms and hands ungloved >
and although garbed in blue and red and pink all at once,
the effect was not vulgar—only Oriental. She did make
a pretty picture.
On dit that a recent presentation by all the lost souls
attached to a big Sydney newspaper to a member, of the
firm who was about to marry was done in an extra-dark
way and was evidently a long-drawn-out torture to the
“ happy ” recipient, who looked all the while as if he
were being tried for manslaughter. The presiding
Patriarch was all to blame, opening his epithalamium:
“ Dear friends, some people say that our birth is the most
important event of our lives; others again yield that
place to Death, but to my idea marriage is far the most
serious of the three.”
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
A queer mixture of society and wharf sightseer as-
sembled at Circular Quay on 11th inst. to see Mrs. Burde-
kin and other friends off by the Orotava, and several
launches hung about in readiness to follow the steamer to
the Heads. Innumerable floral compliments were paid to
the pretty ex-Mayoress, while much popping of corks in
the saloon and cheering was deemed a suitable send-off
for the ex-Mayor. While in England Mr. and Mrs. Bur-
dekin will make a short stay with Lord and Lady
Carington.
r lhe marriage of Mr. Geoffrey E. Fairfax, second son of
Mr. James Fairfax, of the Sydney Morning Herald, to
Miss Hixson, eldest daughter of Captain Hixson, R.N.,
took place on 7th inst. at St. Andrew’s Cathedral, Sydney,
which was florally decorated for the occasion. The bridal
garments were of white armure royale over a jupe of white
satin brocaded, with lillies of the valley, sleeves of puffed
white chiffon, the long tulle veil held by a -diamond star,
hive white bonnet. The bridesmaids were in ,white Ben-
saline and chiffon, with white hat and feathers and posies
of chrysanthemum-autumn leaves. The bride’s going-
away gown was of grey tweed. Mr. and Mrs. Geoffrey
Fairfax left for Europe by the Orotava on 11th inst.
During the race week, Mrs. Bland Holt gave several
ladies’ luncheon parties at her hotel—the last one happen-
ing on 10th inst. A compact number sat down to the
table (artistically decorated with pink and white cosmea
and ferns) and a most enjoyable morning was spent.
Mrs. Bland Holt (who made a tireless hostess) wore a pale
mushroom gown with vest and rekvements of brown silk.
The Minerva Dramatic Society tendered a farewell per-
formance to Mrs. L. D. Hage, prior to her departure for
Rockhampton, at “ Oak wood,” Forest Lodge, the resi-
dence of Mrs. Dwyer, on 7th inst. “Shipmates” was
played by the society in the theatre attached to the
house, after which the company adjourned to the local
Town Hall, where dancing was kept up till a late hour.
“ Shipmates” is a thin play, but amateurs too often pro-
voke unfavourable comparison by the selection of high-
class and popular works. However, the performance all
round was finished and intelligent, and Mrs. Hage, Miss
May Dawson, Messrs. J. Davis and E. J. Redman gave
evidence of the possession of more than ordinary abi ity.
Among recent weddings are those of Miss Hope Lake,
step-daughter of Mr. W. H. Paling, to Captain Proctor,
of Torquay, Devon, and Miss Ida Mann, of North Shore,
to Mr. Lungley, of Adelaide.
THE GREAT REMEDY FOR PAIN.
ST. JACOBS OIL.
WHAT IT IS.
SIX STANDARD VIRTUES.
Ist, It is in one word a cure ; it is not merely
a relief, and in no sense a cure-all; it is the pro-
duct of scientific research.
2nd. It strengthens while it soothes and sub-
dues, heals, and cures ; it literally conquers pain.
3rd. Its effects are curative and permanent to
the whole group of muscular miseries and nervous
agonies.
4th. It does not merely irritate the outer sur-
face, nor does it merely soften or relax a con-
stricted muscle. To its specific action-a superior
curative virtue is superadded.
sth. It penetrates deeply but gently ; search-
ingly and surely seeking the pain spot in an effort
to conquer.
Gth. Each constituent of the formula has a
recognised intrinsic virtue to serve most surely
the cure of pain.
For rheumatism, neuralgia, sprains, bruisesi
stiff joints, swellings, and similar ailments, St-
Jacobs Oil has positively no equal if used accord"
ing to the directions which accompany each bottle-
Aches and pains succumb in a truly marvellous
manner to the potent influence of this world-
famed remedy. Thousands of testimonials have
from time to time been published in the Australian
press—testimonials from all ranks and conditions
of men—from statesmen, judges, eminent divines,
barristers, the medical profession, merchants,
athletes, &c., and though a large standing reward
has been offered to any one who could produce
proof that any one of these was not strictly
genuine, none have dared to question their authen-
ticity. Under these circumstances is it to be
wondered at that the sale of St. Jacobs Oil greatly
exceeds the combined sales of all other advertised
lotions, liniments, embrocations, &c., in the world ?
No home can afford to be without a bottle of it
always handy, and purchasers would do well to
observe that they get the genuine article and not
one of the many spurious preparations made up to
trade upon the enormous reputation which St.
Jacobs Oil has built up for itself upon the solid
foundation of merit.

SUICIDE OR NO SUICIDE.
The New York Life Insurance Company
Fays Its Policy.
It being evident that most cases of self-destruc-
tion involve causes not under the victim’s own
control, and that accident and murder may easily
give rise to a suspicion of voluntary death, the
New York Life Insurance Company, in order to
prevent wrongs to the unfortunate, makes its
policies absolute security. It exercises caution in
granting a policy, and never issues one unless its
officers consider the applicant a good risk. But
when it has once issued its policy and taken the
premium, it feels bound to insure. It does not
insert in its policies the usual clause making them
void in case of “self-destruction, voluntarily or
involuntarily, sane or insane,” and claims no re-
lease from its contract except where there is
evidence of a purpose to defraud. In short, so
far as a life policy may pi-operly be such, the New
York Life’s policies are incontestable from the
moment the first premium is paid. The man with a
New York Life Insurance policy may feel sure that,
incase of his death, no delay, litigation or loss will
arise from the question of self-destruction —
whether it be accidental or otherwise. If he has
been honestly insured, and honestly conforms to
the simple requirements of the contract, the pay-
ment of his' policy is sure on proof of his death.
Chief offices, department of Australasia, corner o'
George and Bond streets, Sydney.

Commercial Depression.
Dear Bulletin,-— After carefully reading the article
headed “ Commercial Depression ” which appears in The
Bulletin, March 7, 1891, in my opinion it can only be
summarised thus: All capitalists are a nuisance to
society. All employers of labour should be hanged. To
my mind, a more grotesque absurdity could hardly be
imagined, if there is such a thing in this world. The
Bulletin, might reply with its usual outspokenness, “ we
write articles, but we do not find brains to read them.”
These remarks would not apply in the present case, as the
following illustration will show. Supposing a digger who
has been working - on a gold-field for a number of years
bad accumulated sufficient capital to allow of him going
out prospecting. After several years he discovers pay-
able gold, and at the conclusion of his operations he
becomes the owner of £lO,OOO. He now becomes a
nuisance to society because he is a capitalist. With the
£lO,OOO he starts business as a manufacturer. Therefore,
as an employer of labour, he ought to be hanged. A few
weeks ag - o a deputation waited upon Sir S. W. Griffith
when it was shown that 1000 men were out of employment
in Brisbane. These unemployed men, like The Bulletin,
denounced all capital, and according to speeches
and manifestoes, are, trying to crush capita), and
drive it out of the country. A strange comment upon
the intelligence of the working-classes in this age of
progress. Again, the article states a person cannot culti-
vate the soil or enter into manufacturing pursuits because
he has to pay rent. If this position holds good in
England and other parts of the world, it certainly is not
the case in Queensland. If a man is inclined to settle on
the land he can do so by getting 150 acres orf payment
of the survey-fee, while a manufacturer could purchase
a piece of land just outside Brisbane for a few pounds,
where he could erect a factory large enough to last to
meet all requirements for the next 100 years. Again,
under that prince of humbugs’ system, Henry George,
the people would have to pay rent to the State instead
of paying it to private ownership. So that according to
the writer’s own showing, neither agriculture nor manu-
facturing industries could be carried out at all unless the
person interested could acquire land rent free. Surely
there is something wrong in a statement of this kind.
Take my own case. lam a manufacturer in a small way,
rent free, residing in one of the best business centres of
Brisbane, and yet I am only getting a bare existence.
And my case is the same with others nearly all over the
to ony. Does this show that in order to become prosper-
ous in business it is only necessary to be rent free ? The
cause of this state of affairs will be seen in the following—
(1.) A rotten system of Government, backed up
by men with no principle, whose main object is to grab
£3OO a year. 2. Borrowed money, for building political
railways and giving work to the unemployed, which show
a dead loss of £500,000 a year, which hangs like millstones
rou id the necks of labour right throughout the colony.
3. The curse of Freetrade, which, instead of giving the
work and wages to the people of this colony, is sent out
of the colony and divided amongst the workmen of
foreign lands, which leaves the people of Queensland
without the means of subsistence. If The Bulletin
would only use its wonderful power and try and destroy
the above evils, I feel sure that it would gain the grati-
tude of the vast majority of thinking people in Queens-
land.—Yours respectfully, A. Ockelford. ' Brisbane.
* * *
In reply to the above eloquent letter, The
Bulletin has only to say that it cheerfully accepts
the dilemma upon which the writer impales it in
his opening paragraph—the individual capitalist
is a nuisance. And as The Bulletin has asserted
this fact in the plainest language and the largest
type for a good many years, it feels but little
flattered that even one of its readers should have
been so long in discovering its meaning. The
individual capitalist is a nuisance and a curse, and
the natural order of things is Co-operation, not
Capitalism. It is no more a part of the scheme
of Creation that one man should work for another
than that one man should belong to another. The
existing system began with the invention of per-
sonal slavery, and the first man who gave up his
independence to toil for another man’s profit was
the serf, who bought and sold and bar-
tered and exchanged like a mere beast of
burden. Any system by which the labourer has to
surrender half or three-fourths of the produce of
his labour in order to pay for the privilege of re-
ceiving the remainder is a glaring absurdity, and
but for the petrifying influence of countless ages
of habit and precedent it could not endure for a
single day. The capitalist who runs this system
is a slave-holder and a dealer in the bodies and
souls of his fellow-men as truly as ever was the
planter of Virginia or the feudal noble of the
Middle Ages. His accumulated wealth —that
which he invests and lives upon without personal
toil or exertion —is not merely money, for money
is only valuable as a representation of wealth ;
it consists of land which he does not require for
his own occupation, but which he holds so that
other men must pay him for standing room on the
earth which God made, and in human beings who
are denied the right to live except at his pleasure
and on his conditions. The earth, the air, and the
sea were given for the equal use of all mankind,
and the capitalist who denies to another man the
use of the earth might as reasonably charge him
for the use of the atmosphere and supply him with
breath through a meter at so much the thousand
cubic feet. And as the whole modern system of
Capital depends, directly or indirectly, on the
great land monopoly, the capitalist is a nuisance,
a usurper, and a curse.
•Jt* •Ji'
Another clause of our correspondent’s letter is
worthy of attention. He says
Supposing a digger, who has been working on a gold-
field for a number of years, had accumulated sufficient
capital to allow of him going out prospecting. After
several years he discovers payable gold, and at the con-
clusion of his operations he becomes the owner of ±IO,COO.
He now becomes a nuisance to society because he is a
capitalist.
We reply briefly that he does. He is a nuisance
through the working of a nefarious system wh'ch
allows him very little chance of being anything
but a nuisance, and even if he wishes to be any-
thing else, it is his misfortune that cno man
cannot abolish the existing system of slaveiy, any
more than one man could abolish the old system
of personal serfdom. In the first place, it is
almost an impossibility that he should have
acquired £lO,OOO by his own unaided exertions.
He must have been in a position, as capitalist or
monopolist, to levy embargo on other mens
toil in order to become possessed of such
a sum. Then, if he invests his money in
the purchase of land he proceeds to monopoli e
the earth, which no man has any right to buy or
sell, and lie helps to deprive coming generations
of their birth light, even as he was deprived of his
birthright by the generation which went before
him. If he invests his capital in a bank or a
building society, he lives partly by land-stealing
and par tly by usury. If Ire spculates in Govern-
ment bonds, he assists in a plan by which men
make unjust wars and fill the earth with misery,
or else build public works at ruinous prices, rtr
order to assist in land swindles and booms and all
manner of public frauds, and beggar posterity to
pay the bill. He is part of a great plan which is
rotten rh every part, and while it endures he is a
nuisance ; and, therefore, The Bulletin has con-
sistently denounced the whole scheme on which
modern society is constructed, and has preached
Socialism as the one available remedy for an evil
which is otherwise incurable.-
# # *
Our correspondent then follows Ins digger a step
further :
With the £lO,OOO he starts business as a manufacturer.
Therefore, as an employer of labour, he ought to be
hanged.
In other words, he has already acquired far more
than his fair share of the world’s wealth, and he
still wants more. His good fortune having placed
it in his power to take advaxAage of other men’s
necessities, by confiscating a certain proportion of
the produce of their labour, he proceeds—being
already rich —to make himself richer and to make
other men poorer, and because he does this he
claims to be a benefactor of his species. He
“affords employment,” which is an inverted way
of expressing the fact that other men are com-
pelled to afford him a certain percentage of their
wealth, and if he fails to make money despite
these advantages it is because he has to compete
with other cormorants, some of them larger and
more rapacious than himself, whereupon his failure
is set down to the wickedness and general infamy
of Labour. But still The Bulletin does not say
that this particular capitalist ought to be hanged.
What it did and does assert is that the entire
army of capitalists, land-holders, and usurers have
established a system whereby the man without
capital is dependent for existence on his employer ;
that this army, having, for its own purposes,
monopolised all the sources of wealth, is morally
bound to find employment for the whole human
race as the only excuse for its own existence and
for the privileges which it enjoys; and that for
every man who dies of hunger in all the cities of
the earth some monopolist ought to be hanged—
so long as murder is regarded as a capital offence
—if only the right monopolist could be found.
Recognising, however, that among so much vicari-
ous responsibility the ri[>ht monopolist seldom
can be found, it does not propose to hang any-
body ; but still it cannot suppose that the God
whom the pious capitalist worships sends any
human being into this world without providingsorne
means for his sustenance, and when those means
are not there when he arrives, it presumes that
some one stole them, and, having thus doomed a
fellow-being to death by slow starvation, became,
from a moral standpoint, a homicide. And if this
theory be not correct, then God has badly botched
His universe, and Providence is a failure.
* * #
Our correspondent further complains that
according to the system of “ that prince of hum-
bugs, Henry George,” every man will pay rent to
the State, and therefore things will stand very
much where they are. To this it is hardly worth
replying. Under the system of the future men
will pay rent to the State only ; whereas now they
pay to the State and the municipality and the
“divisional board ” or the “shire council” under
the name of taxes, and to the landlord under the
name of rent; and the difference between these
two situations is plain to the meanest compre-
hension. Under the new rtc/ime, too, the purpose
of labour will be to support the labourer and not to
enrich the capitalist, and all work will be on the
co-operative principle ; so that when there is not
enough employment to go round, the hours of toil
will be reduced, instead of a certain number of
men continuing to work and the remainder being
consigned to misery and starvation. Then
charity and benevolence will be abolished, for a
system of universal insurance will make provision
for everybody in old age—not by any policy of
loud-mouthed philanthropy, but because it is as
much the duty of the State to abolish the disease
of poverty as it is to wipe out the disease of
drunkenness or homicide; and the mere acquisi-
tion and hoarding of money will be no moie a
virtue than the acquisition of one's fellow-
creatures in avowed slavery. And till that time
comes the capitalist will continue to be a
“ nuisance,” and the individual who, like our
correspondent, is “a manufacturer in a small
way,” or in any other way, will continue to write
letters in defence of the system under which he
tries to prosper and generally does prosper; and
the man who is filled with a good dinner will
refuse to believe in the existence of hunger, and
this world will continue to be the best of all
possible worlds—for the dog that is uppermost.

An Enterprising Queensland Colonist.
MR. THOMAS SHELSWELL. ROMA.
, r Tumi \s Shelswell, the subject of our
Mb- r , - a f a i r sample of a truly repre-
illusfcration, ' , j Bom in Warwickshire
sentative f]ays were
tofarming' pursuits whereby he readily
devoted knowled „ e regarding the
pSa of sheep, horses and cattle wh.ch have
served him so well in the land of Ins adoption,
But Warwickshire farming was a trifle too slow
for Mr. She!well’s ideas _ of success, and, likt
many other young men, with a view to bettering
his state he struck out for London. Here
Iig remained two yGtirs in tlio tiucle,
but about that time some glowing accounts
reached England respecting Queensland as a new
colony for settlement, and Mr. Shelswell deter-
mined to go out and see for himself what the
colony was like. Leaving a young wife in
England he sailed direct for Brisbane and arrived
there in 1809. To be “up and doing ” were the
thoughts uppermost in his mind, and, as nothing
better offered, he procured a digger’s “ kit ” and
made for the Gympio ilns proved
barely a “tucker” field, and hearing that things
were more flourishing in Victoria his first con-
sideration was how to get there with profit and
advantage. By a man of resource such a matter
was easily decided. Mr. Shelswell took steamer
to Sydney, proceeded to the Liverpool Plains,
purchased. a large mob of unbroken horses at
prices as low as 4s. (id. per head (many of them
brumbies), travelled them to Victoria, sold, and
made a very good spec, indeed. His next step was
as timber contractor supplying all heavy forest
woods to the railway contractors for the Worth
Eastern line in the neighborhood of Euroa and
near the river Murray. This completed, Mr.
Shelswell could not resist his old love for farming
and grazing pursuits, and during the early days
of Victorian selection he took up the usual com-
plement of 320 acres which with purchased land
soon totted up to close on 1000 acres - a very good
estate, the size of Victoria being considered. This
was in the neighborhood of Euroa, one of the most
fertile districts in the Murray Valley. Here Mr.
Shelswell followed sheep-farming, cattle-grazing
and cattle-dealing for 10 or 12 years with fair suc-
cess. Having received a good offer for his farm
he let it go, and, returning to Queensland, he
purchased a cattle-station in the Darling Downs
district near Dulacca, in 1882. Thus was a b
the time of one of the worst droughts Queensland
ever encountered, but, nothing daunted, Mi.
Shelswell turned his attention towards business
pursuits and purchased in 1884 a sound butchering
establishment in Roma which he worked in con-
nection with the cattle-run, selling the latter the
following year. His next and probably most sup-
cessful Queensland move was to purchase within
two miles of Roma the compact Estate of I air-
view,’’which with selected and purchased land,
makes about 2000 acres worked in conjunction
with a rented property of G40 acres. Fair view
occupies one of the healthiest sites in the neighbor-
hood of Roma, and irrespective of the genuine
hospitality of its popular proprietor is remarkable
for its extensive richness of pasture and the large
number of sleek, well-fed, fat cattle grazing there-
on. Here a sight meets the eye of the visitor,
and one which very few portions of Queensland
can boast. It consists of a large number of
beautiful black-polled Aberdeenshire cattle, many
of them being first crosses but carrying all the
characteristics of the pure breed. This is a case
in point where Mr. Shelswell has made consider-
able advance as compared with his central Queens-
land neighbours. “Not for me,” remarked the
proprietor of Fairview, “I decided to have a
distinct breed of cattle whose markings I can swear
to,” a matter not so easily managed when one is
confronted with innumerable heads of roans,
“baileys” and “reds.” In connection wit i
grazing and cattle-dealing, Mr. Shelswell still
carries on his butchering establishment in Roma
and is a live-stock judge of the first order as all
business people with whom he lias been associated
will testify. Mr. Shelswell is a man full of energy
and enterprize, great force of character, robust
constitution, genial and kindly disposition and
always with an eye open to the “main chance.
“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at Sydney, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly-Australia's Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin^”
Premier Australian Literary Join ual,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dts
tinctive Australian National Pokey.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
Payment of Members.
One Person, one Vote.
State Revenue derived directly from the Land.
Comolete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
The Bulletin denounces —
Religious Interference in Politics.
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become a vast property, because it pos-
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily ami
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while I he Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form —printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the .Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress : it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal wliich, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast massof original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

A Queensland Civic Magistrate.
MR. FREDERICK BOURNE, J.P., MAYOR OF ROMA.
Mr. Fred. Bourne, J,P., twice Mayor of Roma, was
born at Seaham, Hunter Rtver district, N.S.W., in
1841, and is one of a family of 12, of which Mr.
Alfred Bourne, one of the oldest and most res-
pected citizens in Sydney is the head. Mr.
Alfred Bourne, senr. (by the way, a fellow-
passenger of Sir Henry Parkes), arrived in
Sydney in 1832 by the ship Strath fieldsaye,
and almost immediately proceeded to Seaham.
After a short stay there he decided to settle at
Stroud, Port Stephens district, where he occupied
a farm in connection with the A.A. Co., and
where his family obtained the advantages
of a fair education. Here Mr. Frederick
Bourne spent his early years in farming,
stock-keeping, and general station work, in
which all_ Australian natives about that time took
a hearty interest. The knowledge thus acquired
enabled Mr. Bourne to strike out on his own
account, when he visited the Darling Downs, and
was connected with station work in various
responsible positions, and for nine years was over-
seer at Mount Hutton, a cattle and sheep station of
some magnitude in the Leichhardt district. About
12 or 13 years ago Roma was a promising settle-
ment full of life and activity, so much so that
Mr. Bourne decided to “pin his faith” to the
town and district. The result was that he
started several important industries. It is
not every town that has the good fortune to
have men of Mr. Bourne’s stamp taking an active
part in its progress and prosperity. Mr. Bourne
accepted aldermanic honours about eight years
ago and is now in the second year of his
mayoralty.
In this sketch it may be mentioned that whilst
Mr. Fred Bourne is the eldest of four brothers,
the late M.P., Alfred Bourne, junr., was the
youngest —a young man of great literary promise,
who, in the commencement of a most brilliant
career, was cut down in 1884 after a short illness.
Respecting Mr. Alfred Bourne, junr,, a few
words may be here apropos, inasmuch as for a
young man his literary ability was greatly admired
by all leading men of letters. Having served on
the typographical staff of the S.M. Herald, he
obtained a good appointment in the Govern-
ment Printing Office, and whilst the official his-
tory of N.S. Wales, 1788 to 1883, was being pro-
duced Mr. Alfred Bourne, junr., was selected for
the task of “ Extracting matter for the Epitome
from the official records and compiling in its
present shape.” How well the work was per-
formed a reference to the official history will
amply testify. Mr. Fred Bourne has two brothers,
one with Messrs. Clark Bros., East Talgai, and
another in business at Tamworth. The head
of the family, Mr. Alfred Bourne, senior.,
now about 76 years of age, is one of the most
popular and highly-respected citizens in Sydney.
Answers to Correspondents.
“Walter Goodwin”: Your personal remedy
seems to be to avoid law. You have probably mixed up
the affirmation business. You should also have had a
written agreement and stood on safe ground. You can
appeal personally to Gormly, M.L.A., and your supplica-
tion will assuredly be pigeon-holed ; departmental
reports will outweigh your statements. The address of

Lethe.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Through the noiseless doors of Death
Three passed out, as with one breath.
Two had faces stern as Fate,
Stamped with unrelenting hate.
One upon her lips of guile
Wore a cold, mysterious smile.
Each of each unseen, the pale
Shades went down the hollow vale
Till they came unto the deep
River of Eternal Sleep.
Breath of wind, or wing of bird,
Never that dark stream hath stirred ;
Still it seems as is the shore,
But it flows forevermore
Softly, through the meadows wan
To the Sea Oblivion.
In the dark, like drops of blood,
Poppies hang above the flood,
On its surface lies a thin
Ghostly web of mist, wherein
All things vague and changing seem,
As the faces in a dream.
Two knelt down upon the bank
And of that dark water drank.
But the Third stood by the while,
Smiling her mysterious smile.
Rising up, those shades of men
Gazed upon each other, then
Side by side, upon the bank,
On a bed of poppies sank.
“ What,” one to the other saith,
“ Sent thee through the doors of Death ? ”
“ While life throbbed in ev’ry vein,
For a woman I was slain.
Love is but a fleeting spell,
Hate alone remembers well.
For my slayer I shall wai l,
And though he at Heaven’s gate
•Stand, and wear an angel’s crown,
I shall seize and drag him down ! ”
So the stern shade made reply.
Then the first that spake said, “ I
For a woman’s sake, also,
Slew myself—and slew my foe.
Slew myself, that in no shape
He my vengeance should escape,
Till oblivion swallow both :
And I swore a solemn oath
I would—hate remembers well—
t Hunt his spotted soul to hell.
.But I left, ere leave-taking,
’Round her white throat a red ring.
I shall know her —you shall note—
By that red ring ’round her throat.
A Veil I loved my fair, false wife,
And, perchance, in this new life
She may love me—we shall see —
She shall choose ’twixt him and me. ”
Softly did the other sigh
“ My love’s love will never die.
Love is not a fleeting spell—
Love, like hate, remembers well.
Soon—mayhap on this dim shore—■
We shall meet to part no more.”
Then the first Shade spoke and said,
“ In this Kingdom of the Dead
Let us, who so strangely meet,
Pledge each other in this sweet
Water, our revenge to wreak
Side by side, and so to seek,
Side by side, whate’er our fate,
Those we love and those we hate.”
Kneeling on the dim shore then
Side by side they drank again.
And they saw, like drops of blood,
Poppies nodding o’er the flood,
And they gazed upon the thin
Ghostly web of mist, wherein
All things vague and changing seem
As the faces in a dream ;
And, by some enchantment weird,
As they gazed thereon appeared
Unto each, down-bending low,
Form and features of his foe,
For a moment, then were gone,
And upon the meadows wan—
Half in Death and half aswoon—
Shone a pale and spectral moon.
Then these twain rose, drowsy-eyed,
And departed side by side.
But the Woman-Shade the while
Smiled her cold, mysterious smile.
And her beauty made a light
In that realm of pallid night
(Beauty ends not in the grave)
jfike the moon beneath the wave.
Back she flung her hair of gold,
Glowing, gleaming, fold on fold,
Showing—all but these might note—
The red ring around her throat.
But they passed witli cold surprise.
And unrecognising eyes.
Lightly laughed she then, and said
“ In this Kingdom of the Dead
“ Strange the sights that one may see !
There go twain who died for me
“ Seeking, through Creation wide.
For each other—side by side !”
Then she Wove a poppy-crown,
Placed it on her head, and down
On the river’s margin sank—
But not of the water drank—
Saying : “In the world above
Long he tarrieth, my love ;
Here beside this river’s rim
I shall sleep, and wait for Mm.”’
VICTOR J. DALEY.
Dear Bulletin,— Let me tell you a story
apropos to Robert Louis Stevenson’s graphic
account of the willing manner in which the
melancholy Marquesans go more than half way to
meet death. Some years ago I was field-overseer
on a N.Q. plantation. During one crushing, when
we were working double tides in. our endeavour to
get in the heavy crop before the wet season, we
had a gang of Mallicolo boys feeding; the
“ rollers.” These boys were the pick of the plan-
tation, fine, healthy strapping fellows; and as they
had unusually heavy work, viz., carrying bundles
of cane from where the cane-carts left it to■ the
rollers - a distance of about ten yards and all up-
hill—they were given an extra ration and more
tobacco than the regulation allowance. One day
the owner had occasion to soundly rate the chief
of the gang for interfering with a woman belong-
ing to some other Islander, and the fellow turned
sulky. However, he worked on that day, but the
next morning did not turn up, and on my inquiring
from his mates what was up with him, I saw
directly by their tone and manner that something
was wrong as they answered me : “ Him plenty
sick, that fellow, master been make him no good
altogether.” I then went down to his quarters and
found him lying on his back, but on seeing me
enter he turned his head to the wall and would
not answer. I reported to the owner what the
boys had told me, and he came up to see the sick
boy, but, on our entering the hut, the boy showed
such signs of fear, cowering up in a corner of his
bunk and averting his head from us, that the
owner thought it better to leave him. He went
then to the rest of the gang and tried to get some
explanation from them, but although they did
not seem afraid of the boss, all we could get out
of them was: “ By-and-bye, to-morrow, that
fellow ‘Taracca’ (the sick boy’s name) finish.”
Sure enough next evening he did die. The gang
were then very sullen for a day or so; then
another lay up, refused to be comforted, and died
in two days. A third and fourth followed quickly
—just lay up and died from pure cussedness.
Things began to look serious, as these were our
very best boys, and the example was beginning to
demoralise the remainder. So when the seventh
took to his bed, I just went down to his quarters
and had a look at him. There did not seem any-
thing the matter, but he refused to eat or drink.
I got him put on a stretcher in the back room of
my quarters and made him drink a half-pannikin
of 30 o.p. rum, kept him gloriously drunk all day,
and then left him to sleep it off. Next day he
arose with a headache ; I gave him a nip and "sent
him to work. I had no more sick nigs, from his
gang that season, and all the boys firmly believe in
me as a doctor. “My word, you plenty savee,
make ’im boy very good. Suppose you make ’im
all same nother fellow boy ’im no finish.”—
Kookabubba.
Advise your friends to drink Wolfe’s Schnapps.
A descriptive writer in the Queenslander
tells how nearly 18ft of rain fell at Geraldton, and
Johnston river, N.Q., in 1890, and in seven weeks
of this year, 78 inches were recorded there; while,
in 1889, at Goondi, two miles from Geraldton,
198 inches fell. It was from Geraldton that an
application for a boy was made to bale out the
rain-gauge, it being found impossible to regulate
it any other way.
Queensland authorities will try to “ jug”
Vosper, “trustee” of the comically “seditious ”
Republican, for brandishing his tin-dagger, reek-
ing with stage clots incarnadine, and howling for
more vats of gore. The “ seditious ” article is the
jaggedest panorama that ever spurted from a
crank’s brain, and drops in its moods suddenly
from star-heights of bombast to the ludicrous mud-
sprawling of a circus-clown. Its author, in one of
his forked-lightning zigzags, yells that the unionist
shearers “ must have either bread or blood —
WOOL or heads— and if the Government be not
careful they will have both.” Sheep’s jimmies
with the wool on ! Black-puddings and damper !
After an impassioned tally-ho to all “MEN” to
“strike for your rights, your manhood, your
country and your lives,” the astral acrobat falls
headlong to earthy burlesque as thus daubed : — 1
“ If your*oppressors will not listen to reason, let
them feel cold lead and steel; as they have
starved, so do you shoot them; and allow them
not to destroy your liberties and deprive you of
your bread without a fight. Better to see the last
squatter and the last member of this hateful
Government butchered than to see one jot or one
tittle of the sacred rights of the people lost!” The
Bloody Liberator is here supposed to hoof-paw th
slow music and veiled bull’s-eyes, three strides iil
a bar, ricocheting from each wing of the stage,
with nitro-glycerine in his heart and livid light-
ning in his eyes. Seriously, how can you crimi-
nally arraign a burlesque ‘ ‘ sedition ” of this sort 1
Any judge who visited such laughable tommyrot
with the stern penalties of the law would pro-
claim himself utterly devoid of humour.
Many people seeing the words “Microbe-
Killer” have been led to believe that it is a
disinfectant. It is not a disinfectant, but a
powerful medicine, for while it kills the germs of
disease, it does it in a different manner. It is
taken internally or applied externally as the case
may require. It attacks and kills the microbes in
the system which are the direct cause of disease.
After these germs are destroyed the medicine will
thoroughly cleanse the blood and tone up the
whole system—in short, it restores to a healthy
condition all the members of the body. Although
this medicine has only been for sale in Australia
a short time the constantly increasing sales bear
witness to the good it is accomplishing. People
who have been using the Microbe Killer for con-
sumption, kidney and liver troubles, indigestion,
and, in fact, for almost every disease, are loud in
their praises of its virtue. Being composed of
pure water charged with powerful germ destroy-
ing gases it is as pleasant to drink as pure spring
water. Send for the book, “History of Microbes
and the Microbe Killer,” posted free. The head
office is 40 Blizabeth-street, Melbourne, but
agencies have been established at 319 George-
street, Sydney; 17 Hunter-street, Newcastle;
and 26 Queen-street, Brisbane, where the medicine
can be obtained.*

Feminine Morality.
Mrs. Mayo, an English writer, proceeds to argue
in the Leisure Hour that women are distinctly
inferior to men in many important elements of
human character. To begin with, women, she
says, blind themselves to their own wickedness by
using innocent terms to describe plain sins.
Treachery is called tact; lies, fibbing ; drunken-
ness, over-stimulation ; while thoughtlessness is
simply sheer vulgar selfishness and disregard for
the rights of others. Selfishness may be equal in
both sexes, but it manifests itself pre-eminently in
some women lay their capacity for petty pushing
and grasping, and by their callous indifference to
the rights and feelings of others ;
I am afraid that disregard for others’ rights, an indis-
position to look not only on one’s own things, but also on
the things of others, is a besetting weakness of our poor
sex. I think it is John Ruskin who somewhere says, in
his rather sweeping fashion, that he has come across very
few women who seem to realise that there is anybody
else in the world except themselves and their own
children !
Sometimes out of spiteful inquisitiveness, some-
times to gratify a mean vanity, women will
recklessly undermine or damage the influence or
authority which is really responsible for the wel-
fare of young lives. Nor is this all. While men
and women are far more alike than they are dif-
ferent, women are much wickeder in being more
spiteful :
For instance, women go out of their way to inflict pain
or contumely on each other, even when there is no ques-
tion of revenge or spite, or scarcely of personal know-
ledge. I have no doubt some men are quite as purse-
proud or house-proud as some women, but generally they
will simply neglect those whom they outshine. The
woman wishes to drive home her triumph and enjoy it
with the flavour of somebody’s humiliation.
Then, again, few women have among themselves
any share of that which among men is called
“honour.” A room full of girls will indulge in
the most spiteful remarks about a visitor whom
they see coming up the avenue, and then the
moment she has arrived in the room they will
surround her with greetings, hugging her, kissing
her, and generally teaching a terrible lesson as to
the possibilities of feminine treachery :
I have heard men make remarks about others both
spiteful and severe. . . . But those men would not
straightway fawn upon the object of their contempt and
profess an unctuous joy in his society. If any man did
so, his fellows would instantly dub him a cad and a sneak.
And yet too many men only laugh when they detect
women in these mean treacheries! Quite recently a
“society” journal said airily, as a matter of course, that
women are allowed to speak and behave in ways which
would cause the immediate ostracism of a man.' I think
the virtue about which women should examine them-
selves and their ways is truthfulness ! I do think it is the
lack of this which underlies much bad manners, and social
treachery, and petty injustice.
Mrs. Mayo fim'shes up by quoting Canon Little-
dale, who says that men rarely stab one another
with the personal stilettoes of concentrated spite
which women are apt to wield. Men are loth to
strike a fallen enemy, whereas too many wemen
delight in trampling on him to the last. He also
says that there is a lower standard of truthfulness
among women than among men, proved by small
social hypocrisies, continual inaccuracy, and a
marked tendency to exaggeration.
Then another writer in another magazine con-
tinues the music :
It was a lady of title (he remarks) who brought about
the present gambling trouble at Tranby Croft. It is well
known that among card-players in private houses ladies
are notoriously the most persistent and open cheats, and
that they do again and again with complete impunity the
very acts which are accounted disgraceful and ruinous in
a man. It was only recently that, at a card-party, one
htled ! ac| y violently boxed the ears of another and
accused her of cheating. So far, however, from the
acC usation injuring the latter, the laugh went entirely
against the aggressor for getting violently excited about
an everyday occurrence. Nay, more, the explanation given
of this unusual excitement was that* probably the
boxer had herself cheated, and the foul play of the boxed
J lad rol3bed the former of the profits of her own dis-
honest Y- So callous, moreover, is Society on this sub-
jeot that if a one refused t 0 play with these ]adieg nov
0 n the ground that they cheated, he would be regarded as
an imbecile, who neither knew the world nor its manners,
It is recognised that women must cheat if they play at
cards, or, indeed, at any game where cheating is possible,
They can no more help it than they can keep a secret, or
reft-fiiii from tattling, or keep away from men (especially
lf the i a tt er do not want them). They do not realise the
criminality or disgrace of it, and a man might talk him-
self black in the face before he could succeed in persuad-
ing them’that there was any more harm in it than in
abusing a prettier woman than themselves or cutting out
a hated rival. To treat the offence, moreover 'when
committed by a woman as being on a level with cheating
by a man, would really be as rational as to punish a child
for ill-treating a doll in the same measure as one would
mete out to a man kicking his wife,
. In short, in England as elsewhere women are a disturb-
3ng cbment in all preconceived notions or schemes of
“- the worst of the thing is that if any effort
were made to impress the right view on the mind of affair
culprit by harsh measures, she would not only get the
sympathy of a majority of her own sex out of fellow-
feeling, but she would also have the strong support of a
number of the other sex, on the purely illogical ground
that she was pretty, or attractive, or sensitive, or per-
kaps only delicate in health ; all of which excuses, if a
man a ttempted to urge them, would merely be regarded
as an aggravation of her offence. Men, in fact, may con-
sider themselves fortunate if the ladies confine themselves
merely to cheating, for many of the harpies who play
regularly with a determination to wun at any price, not
not only call in dishonest skill to avert the unkindness of
chance, but very often decline to be bound by the decision
0 f the blind goddess, and, in short, refuse to pay as well,

A Murrumbidgee whaler, way back from
Carrathool, declares to The Bulletin, that the
fish in that part of the river are the best in N.S.
Wales, and better than Newfoundland cod.
Dear Bulletin,-— lt is not the unemployed
of the big cities whom the Unionist shearers
despise for co-operating with the pastoralists. It
is the free selector—he who leaves 9, good home,
with a gold ring on his finger, to play the role of
blackleg for filthy lucre which he does not press-
iugly need. That’s the fellow. The man who
despoils his poorer brother of a few pounds in the
hour of the latter’s need.—S.
It is related that in the neighbourhood of
Cooper’s Creek, on St. Patrick’s Day, a Milesian
gentleman, who had collapsed, was left to rest in
the bush whilst his friends carried on the spree.
On awakening he was terrified by his loneliness
and sought refuge in impromptu prayer:—“ O Lad
Cad Almighty, look down upon me, Patrick
Doolan, Esq., Jay Pay of Jumba Station, brother-
in-law to Michael O’Grady, Esq., Jay Pay of
Yarrowlahmine Station, lost in the wilds of this
black Australian hush for ever and ever, Amen.
Glory be to Gad !
A Victorian reporter, after hideously
depreciating Sydney’s dowdy women and “ dirty,
miserable shops, stocked with fifth-rate goods,”
winds up about the gutter gamins thus :
Everywhere the children, even those of parents whom
we should crown in Victoria as belonging to the class of
respectable tradesmen, are shoeless, hungry-looking and
thinly clad, and among the people one meets in the
street-ways there is nothing of that sleek, plenty-to-eat,
shot-in-the-locker look that characterises the Victorian.
A visit to Sydney in the year 1891 is only a saddening
experience.
A number of the Australian daily papers
the other day received a cablegram, probably
originally worded thus :■ —
Influenza Chicago 300 deaths Monday funerals congest
traffic.
The Australian Star, however, obviously has a
special cable-service of its own, for do we not read
in its columns the following :
London, April 1.
The influenza is raging in Chicago with unprecedented
deadliness. The paralysing influences of the terrible
malady have assumed the alarming dimensions of a
plague. The distemper is of a powerfully-destructive
character, and shatters its victims with fearful sudden-
ness. The severity of the visitation eclipses all previous
appearances of this discomforting disease. The medical
faculty of America fails to account for the present out-
break, and is equally powerless to control its spread. The
doctors, nurses and charitable institutions are doing
everything possible for the sufferers, but are overwhelmed
by the multitude crying bitterly for relief from the
tortures of the fatal influenza. A despatch received from
Chicago, where the malady is raging most violently,
states that last Monday’s record was 300 deaths. This,
also, is about the daily average. The city has
been cast from a condition of activity and happy vigour
into indescribable gloom and unbridled terror. The great
streets of the populous city are congested with vehicles
carrying to the sepulchre the silent victims of the visita-
tion. The aspect of the burial-grounds is tragically
mournful and mysterious. Grave-diggers are delving
into the turf night and day, and another company is
ceaselessly engaged shovelling clay on the dead influenza
dust. All the time hearses crowd the cemetery gateways
and coffins of luxurious and humble designs are shoul-
dered about with promiscuous regularity. Hysterical
mourners for the dead are entangled everywhere, and
confusion reigns conjointly with lamentation. The in-
habitants of the city are filled with terror, and the sad
vacancy of death depresses every household. Fears are
entertained that the influenza outbreak has by no means
reached the climax of its furies and terrors, aud that the
next few weeks will show more shocking dimensions of
the visitation.
This kind of thing is an insult to human intelli-
gence, and deceives nobody but the gabbling idiot
who sends it over the ocean at such an alarming
expense to the enterprising Stab proprietary.
They hanged a man in Melbourne the
other day, and there was the usual blab of pathos
in the two evening papers when the affair was
over. The Herald looked at the matter from a
subdued religious standpoint, with a wail in it,
and a dash of left-off prophecy ; and warmed up
the cold hash that was left from the last sermon,
and in a general way, it banged a moral hymn in
a muffled tone of tears and lamentation. But the
Standard wisely spread itself rather on the
artistic aspect of the picture, and dealt with the
fleeting shadows that were cast by the sunlight
upon the gallows, and the rich, dark colouring
and tranquil hang of the noose, and the mellow
tints of the door of the condemned-cell as they
stood out boldly against the profound calm of the
whitewash in the corridor. The following are
typical specimens of these two distinct orders of
literature—the coarsely-daubed snuffle of the
Herald coming first ;
Man, notwithstanding his education and his surround-
ings, still has, to a greater or lesser extent, that jealousy
and that passion which developed in Cain, and led to the
murder of Abel. Now as then man is a ship afloat on an
ocean, and too often, though there may be an attempt to
steer by the best promptings of reason, the gale of passion
proves too strong and drives the ship on to the rocks,
where destruction is inevitable. As it was with Cain, and
has been since with thousands of others, so apparently it
will be until those halcyon days of universal selfrrestraini,
wisdom, and happiness, which poets sing of, and which
the weary storm-tossed would long ardently to see.
After this garish hunk of pious reflection the
Sabbath calm of the other paper is an oasis in the
desert. One sentence will do :
A stray sunbeam played furtively through the roof as
though anxious to see too, and for a moment lit up the
spot where, in another minute, the condemned man would
stand to take his last farewell of every thing .earthly.
There is nothing repulsive about that picture.
The writer doesn’t rise and throw the strangled
corpse at your head in a manner of speaking.
He rather seeks to cast a delicate witchery about
the gallows, and to evoke a morning radiance out
of the hangman, and he finds a solemn, im-
moveable grandeur in the solid repose of,the trap-
door. And if the patient, instead of being exe-
cuted, had had his head knocked off in a tram-
accident, the same author would have told how
the moon rose, like a silver shield over the brains
scattered on the track, and the deathless stars
smiled softly down on the squashed ear of the
deceased. Looking through his story we begin'to
realise how the genius of Byron would have shone
forth if he had gone to report a gaol-flogging, and,
if Milton had only fixed up an account of a hang-
ing when he was in his prime the literature of his
country would have been a good deal richer than
it is. And, even if Tennyson'happened to drop in
at an inquest on a man who had fallen off a
particularly high roof, and put the details about
his banged and damaged fellow-creature into
imperishable verse he might do a lot to lift up the
moral tone of the community.
If people who are troubled with colds will take
Ayer’s Cherry Pectoral before going to church,
they will avoid coughing. The pectoral soothes
and heals the irritated tissues, and controls all
disposition to cough.

Sydney Daily Telegraph : —
Such rights as the masses of any country now possess
have been wrung from reluctant rulers by the constitu-
tional force of majorities after incessant struggle.
This argument evidently rests on theD.T.’s inter-
pretation of the word “constitutional.” Was the
force used by the Magna Charta barons “con-
stitutional?” If so, why hound down the Queens-
land shearers ? Was the Cromwell interregnum
“ constitutional ?” Why, then, execrate the mild
rebellion in the Northern colony? Was the in-
dependence of the United States gained by “ con-
stitutional force?” Yes; by the constitutional
force of unconstitutional but successful rebellion
against despotism. All successful rebellions arc
“constitutional”—after they have succeeded.
Haddon Chambers, we are told by a man
just from England, lives in “slap-up” style in
Clanricarde Gardens, Bayswater. He is as full
of “orders’’for plays as possible, having two in
hand for America and two for London —one for
Mr. John Wood, and the other probably for Tom
Thorne, of the Vaudeville. He is the parent of a
fine baby girl {not a boy, as reported), named
Marjorie. He has entirely ceased to write tales
for the magazines, “ as they don’t pay, anyhow ”
—The Bulletin, by the way, was never much
struck with Haddon C. as a story-writer. His
first literary start in London was with a short
Anglo-Australian story called “Outwitted,” pub-
lished in Society in March, 1884.
There was a breach of promise case on
recently in Christchurch (Maoriland), and, judging
from the evidence, the plaintiff had drivelled more
to the defendant, and the defendant had been
more helplessly maudlin with regard to the
plaintiff, than even the average of their kind.
But when the defendant stated that the plaintiff
had declared “ it would be joy even to faint at
his feet ” the court stopped to laugh. The court
always does laugh on such solemn occasions. Once
when the present writer was a good deal younger
and more attractive than he is now, and when he
had more hair and 100 per cent, less spectacles, a
love-sick maiden told him that even to have fits on
his sideboard would do something to relieve the
pains of unrequited affection that troubled her
weary heart, and most likely if that fact had oonu
before a court, it would have laughed, too. Why
should love’s bright dream be held up to scorn
before a judge and sold for 6s. 8d., and wherefore
are the tenderest emotions of the human soul
dragged before a bench and labelled “ Exhibit A ?’’
In the prisons of Macedonia, when a
convict persistently breaks the gaol regulations,
he is fastened down naked on the floor, and fifty
large ants are shaken out of a bag on top of him.
Then he and the ants are left together for an hour
or more, and at the end of that time he is allowed
to shake the insects off and go back to his work.
The English paper which reports this painful cir-
cumstance feels shocked at the barbarity of the
unspeakable Turk. In an English prison, under
similar conditions, they would humanely flog the
convict till he was nearly insensible and partially
skinned alive; and then he would be sent to the
hospital till his hide grew again; and a pious
Britain naturally feels disgusted at the “ tortures”
applied by the Ottoman gaoler. The great soul of
England is a whale in the matter of sympathy.
One of the saddest stories we remember is that of
a British working-man who broke most of his
wife’s ribs and kicked her teeth down her throat,
because she hadn’t got his supper ready when he
came back from a meeting where they had been
denouncing the Bavarian “atrocities.”
“What town is this?” “Nyngan.”
“ Does anybody live here ? ” “ Rather ! It is the
centre of the real life and intelligence' of N.S.
Wales.” “Is that dilapidated barn there, with
the scoopy roof and the bag-stuffed window-holes,
the Moated Grange ? ” “ That, sir, is the Nyngan
Court-house, where the great palladium of the
people is centred and enshrined.” “Is it mag-
nificently furnished?” “You bet it is. It
possesses three chairs —one for the magistracy,
one for the bar, and one for the press—besides a
dozen broken ones piled up in a corner.” “ How
did they get broken?” “In fair legal fight.”
“ Do you decorate the Court-house on great occa-
sions?” “Oh, yes; and when Nyngan is pro-
claimed the Federal Capital, we are going to
decorate it -with repairs, and new chairs, and,
maybe, panes of glass and a form and a table and
an inkpot, out of the first Federal Judicature
Vote.” “Thank you. And now show me the
shortest route out of this magnificent city.”

Until lately an Australian official Gazette
used to offer so much a head for dingo’s tails.
Our old friend, Giovanni Sala, late of the
Oaloola, Cowflat marble quarries, is at it again.
This time at Los Angeles, in California, where he
has just discovered a petrified man near Fresno,
who, judged from the description, is own brother
to the marble gentleman that drew in so many
shillings in Sydney.
A word on a faded photo. That of the
Victorian cricketers in Sydney, 1859, now draws
much attention in Melbourne. There is only
one survivor of the team, Thornton, who lives at
Camperdown, but rarely misses an “Inter-
colonial ” match. On the other hand, four mem-
bers are alive of the N.S.W, team in 1859.
Sir Thomas Elder’s exploring expedition
will advance into the Northern Territory by a
route between Warburton’s (1873) and Gregory’s
(1850). This will take the party into a tract of
unexplored country between Tennant’s Creek and
the West Australian boundary, and in which
there is thought to be a faint hope of finding relics
of Leichhardt.

Chicago Millionaii’e Lathrop left £2000
last February for a 75ft.-high elm to be moved
12 miles, and put over his body, and they did it.
“ He used to sit under the elm, and now it sits on
him.”
In Hongkong purchases are not often
made with ready cash. The vogue there is to give
a signed order and await the end of the month.
The month’s total can, if necessary, be “paid” by
giving a fresh “chit ’for the lot and tearing up
the other evidences. This explains the yarn about
the Chinaman who, being pressed by his banker
to reduce his over-draft, replied blandly : “All li;
givee blankee cheque, me payee off ovadlaft.”
Black and White, the new rival to the
Graphic and Illustrated London News, is out.
It is a charming paper, but, we fear, too high-
toned for a world in which people live on beef
and beer.
“Blue-ribbon” Clark, Mayor of Brisbane,
declares that, during 22 minutes on a recent Sun-
day morning, he counted 110 men as they entered
a Brisbane pub. That means £1 9s. at the very
least, if the Blue-ribbouman didn’t see multi-
nularly.
A wire from the North states that not one
of the arrested shearers had a penny on him.
Very likely, indeed. The'shearer is not the man
who years ago collared all the beautiful North
Western country for an old song, worked it with
cheap labour and made his immortal pile.
Sherman’s march to the sea, over 300
miles, was accomplished, by the 100,000 men who
composed his force, in 23 days. Wherever he
passed, slavery vanished, and at length the
Federal troops had in their rear from three to four
hundred thousand negroes.

To 11. F. Kugelmanx, Esq.
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, blit I became worse everyday, wasting away until
I became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day,.! became very much alarmed.
Heaving of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sura. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
cr.d of three months I vas taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before rne, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 3SS3.
Peter SurniE, J.p.
